GAME: A Game of Thrones (first edition)
RANK: 310
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
-Plotting your orders for the turn is awesome.
-Bidding for the areas of influence is awesome.
-The theme is awesome.
-The board and bits are awesome.
-Doublethink in battles is awesome.
-The negotiation is awesome.
-Having such limited resources is awesome.
-Both expansions are awesome.
-The incredible tension when you're going for the victory is awesome.
-That it's so easy to teach and play is awesome.
-The game is awesome.

Seriously, it's sex in boardgame form.

RATING: 3
Listen to people who say that if you haven't read the books, don't bother with the game. If theme is important to you (it is to me), then you want to like and know the theme of a game. I'm not into high fantasy so I should have known better.

The mechanics of the game are fairly simple though some don't feel quite right (particularly the bidding portion). It's sort of Risk with elements of Dune and Wallenstein. When all meshed to together, it feels weaker than the individual parts. 

If you aren't into diplomacy then forget about this game. Some positions (houses) are just stronger than others and the only way for the weaker houses to combat that is to gang up or at least have a truce or two going. And the game is unfortunately really l o n g.

RATING: 3
The essential problem with this game is that it's at best mediocre in a field were there a lot of very good or even excellent games.  It simply isn't as good as exciting or as deep as Wallenstein/Shogun, Struggle of Empires, Byzantium, Perikles, Empires of the Ancient World, Antike and several others you can mention.  All those games are similar in that they are comabt games which give you lots of possible actions.  Ok, it may be more demanding and involved than Vinci, but then Vinci is very quick to play and this one drags in at about 3 and half hours.  In fact I'd probably rather do the hoovering than play this, and, alas, I'm told that the expansion makes the game worse not better...that's one to avoid then!!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game got a different feel everytime and the way orders are handled offers a unique strategy experience. The only chance element in the game is through the cards pulled at each round, and this is made in a way so it does not limit the strategy. The bidding on political influence, handling of supply for the army and gain of power tokens, offers a variety of different approaches to the game. Each area has a strategic value, so it is up to the player to focus on the way he wants to go. Then finally the uncertain diplomacy between players gives you the feeling of never being completely safe, even in alliances.

RATING: 9
Awesome book series, awesome game.  It helps if players have read the books but it's not exactly necessary.  Very well balanced game that keeps the powerful in check and does an okay job of keeping the weak in the game.  Intense player vs player action starts almost from the beginning, including lots of informal agreements and alliances across the table.  If one player gets too powerful too quickly then the other players can quickly team up to even things out.  I have only ever played with 4 people, but would love to get a game of 5 (includes Greyjoys) or 6 (Greyjoys & Martells).  Starks are probably the easiest starting house while the Tyrells prove to be the most challenging.  Epic 4-5 hour game where plans are constantly being laid and adjusted.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

RATING: 4.5
Wow, where is the fun is this one?  We couldn't even get through an entire game we were so bored.  It sounded so good in theory, but the gameplay was slow and uninvolving.  The whole supply issue seemed to throttle any build up of forces, and the battles were analytical, lacking any zest, probably due to the lack of randomness.  Meanwhile, the Westerone cards were too random--we turned up 4 supply cards in a row and were halfway through the game before a Mustering card came up--far too late in the game.  The Wildings attacks were rare and pathetic, not nearly so much trouble as a lone gnat flying by.  Our group agreed we give it another try in a couple of months, but we were pretty disenchanted on this first go-round.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played this for the first time with 5 players - too sleepy, not sure what was going on. Played this a second time as House Stark. Won the game (although we only played 9 rounds since we had to leave) with more supplies in a tie with 5 cities in competition with the yellow player. The problem with my position is that I felt super isolated from everyone else. The only front that made sense for me to expand into is the black one, since the empty area between me and yellow seemed  too far with little incentive to go there with a city at the furthest point. And I still felt like it didn't make sense for me to battle until the seventh round, which left me on my own for most of the game. The other issue we had was that the luck of the event cards meant we had all our supply actions first (one 3 army, three 2 armies for the whole game) with musters coming up in the latter middle part of the game, which meant conflict was pretty dry in the first half of the game. This game has similar mechanics to Shogun, but Shogun feels way more balanced. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good game but has some balance problems.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A really solid offering in the empire building, conquest type game department. I've only played with 5 players and I'm not sure how balanced the game would be with less. I love the tension in choosing actions for your armies. The card driven battles are simple but very effective and the uniqness of each factions cards is a nice touch. The auctions for turn order and the other abilities adds another nice layer of decisions to the game. An all around great game that is limited only  by the fact that it seems to work best with only 5 players and that it's an old school type conquest game and so probably has limited appeal among more conventional eurogamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
25 Jan 07:  Great game of strategy, diplomacy, and cunning, with enough randomness thrown in to ensure no two games will be the same!  Only drawback is that ships can be too powerful--but this is solved with the Ports rule from the ACoK expansion!  With that expansion, this game becomes a 9 for me!

RATING: 6
http://www.unknowns.de/wbb2/thread.php?postid=43643#post43643
[b]6. Spieler inklusive neue Regionenverteilung im Süden[/b]
Sehr schön, zu sechst spiele ich am liebsten, und es verändert die gesamte Balance des Spiels.
[b]Neue Hauskarten[/b]
In beiden Erweiterungen gibt es neue Hauskarten. Wir spielen am liebsten mit den Karten aus der 1. Erweiterung, die generell nicht so stark schwanken, wie die aus dem Grundspiel und die aus der 2. Erweiterung.
[b]Offenliegende Westeros-Karten der Folgerunde[/b]
Diese Regeloption ist auch leicht ohne Kauf einer Erweiterungsbox einzuführen.
[b]Häfen[/b]
Dämpfen stark das Problem, das man praktisch keine neuen Schiffe mehr bauen kann, wenn man die Herrschaft über die eigene Küstenregion verloren hat. Außerdem sind sie einfach ein schönes Element.
[b]Festungen[/b]
Sind nett, aber kein Muß.
[b]Kriegsmaschinen[/b]
Sind nett, aber kein Muß. Machen das Spiel nach meinem Gefühl aber offensiver - wer eine Kriegsmaschine hat, setzt sie auch ein...
[b]Spezialbefehle[/b]
Eher nicht.
[b]Taktikkarten[/b]
Diese Option mag ich sehr, sie hilft, eine generelle Linie vorzugeben und erleichtert so die Wahl, welche Befehle ich nun diese Runde legen will.
[b]Alternative Westeros-Karten[/b]
Wichtig eher für die 4-Spieler-Variante
[b]Wildling-Karten[/b]
Keine Meinung
[b]Anführer[/b]
Unschlüssig - ein sehr nettes Spielelement und vor allem in Verbindung mit Geiselnahme - Verhandlungen zur Freilassung - Hinrichtung sehr schön zur Spielwelt passend, für viele andere aber nur viel Aufwand ohne großen Nutzen (vgl. die Diskussion Ameritrash/Eurogames).

RATING: 5
Diplomacy with added bells in the form of the leaders from Dune and whistles in the form of random events and some added complexity. A genuine flavour of the George R.R. Martin novels is sadly lacking.  The limited number of turns was initially appealing (a multi-player wargame that will definitely finish in three hours!), but ultimately unsatisfying as it encourages very Kingmakerly tactics.  

FFG did a good, pre plastic miniatures with everything, job of the production.  The artwork is functional though uninspired.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Kind of a bloated version of Diplomacy that is very unbalanced. Playing as Highgarden is a guaranteed defeat, while most games seem to end in a Baratheon victory, regardless of the player.

  Issue orders to troops through tokens on their locations and hope that you aren't going to get attacked from all sides, the combat cards work well and give the battles more dynamics and can give some wonderful surprise victories.

  I like the way you must balance supply and army size, the number of barrels you control dictates the sizes of your various forces which works well, or would if the board was more balanced.

  I haven't yet played 2nd edition so not sure how much that is improved, but there is certainly room for improvement here. Mechanics work very well, but the board is unbalanced.

RATING: 3
60€ is too much for a playtest version. The game is broken without at least one of the expansions. Ships are too strong, levies are left at the whims of fate, takes too long to complete and the rules are a mess. As usual FFG lacks both in the rules and the playtest departments.

You can fix some of those aspects in the game by paying 40€ to buy the Clash of Kings expansion. Sorry guys but for 100 euros i can buy at least 2 games or a big box set like runewars which is a gazillion times better.

As a diplomacy clone, it pales before the likes of Dune, Wallenstein and Shogun. 

In a few words: a game for fanboys of Martin, not gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is one of my favorites and the best multi-player war game I've played.

I give it a '10', but it does honestly require some tweaking to get there.  It can be a little long sometimes, but you can easily reduce the number of turns played.  It's also possible for some houses to really get screwed on the first turn if you don't start with the right opening moves -- but if everyone understands those dynamics it's not an issue.

There's a slight chance that the Westeros cards don't provide crucial events (like mustering new troops) for several turns in a row.  The uncertainty of what will happen next is vital to the game, but it can go too far.  The solution is to modify the Westeros decks.  For instance, we set up the first deck with 1 Mustering, 1 Supply, 1 Nothing Happens, and 1 Reshuffle-Redraw -- but if Nothings Happens comes up we borrow from the ACOK expansion and allow the holder of the Iron Throne to choose between Muster, Supply, and Nothing Happens.  The other three decks are constructed similarly.  This all but eliminates the problem and adds another incentive to hold the special tokens.

Finally, it doesn't support 4 very well right out of the box.  But it can easily be turned into a fantastic four player game.  The first step is to eliminate Pyke -- pretend it doesn't exist -- to prevent the Lannisters from easily gaining too much power.  We also typically give the Tyrell's a second starting ship to allow him to be more competitive in the southern seas.

So yeah, I've only talked about what's bad and how to fix it.  And it is unfortunate that it requires this extra work.  But it's worth it because it turns into one of the best games of its kind.

I'll have to be brief about what's good.  The core is everyone simultaneously placing placing secret actions.  It's crucial that you anticipate what everyone else will do so that you move, defend, raid, and support in the right places.  But you're not just guessing because the game thrives with negotiations between players -- forming alliances and back-stabbing.

There's no luck in combat, but there's plenty of uncertainty.  You can never be sure who will support who during the battle, and you can never be sure how many bonuses from their cards each player will commit to win.

And finally you a good political system working on the side.  Players that can gather more political power gain an obvious advantage when bidding that political power to take hold of special abilities that can swing the balance of military power on the map.  It all really works together well.

RATING: 7
This game has been compared to Diplomacy.  While the 2 games share some concepts, the play of the two is very different.  Diplomacy feels like posturing, backroom deals, and betrayal.  aGoT feels like wickedness, deceit, and dishonesty.  The game captures the distrust and jockeying of the major houses in Westeros.  Players must work with each other to gain (or contain) advantage.  But all the while it happens, everyone knows that it will not end well.  aGoT is unlikely to end friendships, but it is also unlikely to foster close bonds with your fellow players.  Recommended for game groups with tight ties and thick skin.  There will be conflict.  It will feel personal.  

But in the end, it's just a game.  And the game aspects work quite well.  Turns are fluid and players stay engaged throughout each turn.  Downtime is not very high because everyone is watching what unfolds.  Like the series (book and show) - it is an engaging experience.

RATING: 7
This is a great game.  It is simple, elegant, and driven primarily by strategy, diplomacy, and number-crunching.  The only random element is the decks of event cards, and they affect everyone more or less equally.  The simultaneous orders is a brilliant game mechanic, and the areas of influence are exceptionally balanced, especially in a 5-player game.

Since writing this comment, 2nd Edition has completely replaced the original.  2nd Ed. is equal or superior in every category, so I'll never play this one again.  Trade, anyone?  I have both expansions!

RATING: 6
Játszani még nem játszottam vele, de az tuti, hogy a doboza, és a kinyomók is szuperek. Profi kivitelezés, remélem a játékban sem csalódok.
---
Nos, a játék nem okozott katarzist, de nem rossz. Olvastam kritikaként, hogy meglátszik rajta, hogy 2003-as, akkor talán nagyobbat ütött, de ma már túlhaladottá vált. Én leginkább az eredetiséget hiányolom belőle, egyébként jól össze van rakva. A mechanizmus alapjait szerintem a Diplomacy-ban kell keresnünk, csak itt van olyan parancs is, amivel hatalompontokat lehet szerezni (ami sok mindenre jó). De egyébként a támadás, támogatás, szállítás, visszavonulás eléggé hasonló, mint a bő 40 évvel korábbi ősében... A játékban elég hamar előállhat olyan helyzet, amikor valakinek nincs esélye a győzelemre, mert a sok vár jelenti a győzelem feltételt és az újabb seregeknek is előfeltétele. Ahhoz hogy győzzek, vár kellene, amit el kellene foglalni, de amíg nincs váram, seregem se lesz több... ciki. Ami még hátrány, hogy kicsit monotonná válhat a játék. De a hossza kellemes meglepetés, azzal riogattak néhányan, hogy nagyon elnyúlhat, nekünk a 4 fős 2 és fél óra volt. Még egyszer mondom, nem rossz, néha szívesen leülök vele játszani, főleg hogy a sorozat tetszett, de nem átütő.
Akinek ez tetszett, az a Senjit is próbálja ki feltétlenül (bár ott nem csak harccal lehet nyerni), meg persze a Diplomacyt.
Hallottam olyat is, hogy a Riskhez hasonlították, de szerintem ennyi erővel a Shogunhoz is lehetne hasonlítani, és az kevésbé lenne degradáló.
Egy fontos dolgot elfelejtettünk a játékunk során: egyáltalán nem tárgyaltunk egymással, pedig ez nincs tiltva, és színt vitt volna a játékba.

RATING: 5
A Game of Thrones probably appeals more to gamers who enjoy making/breaking alliances with neighboring players, such as is the case in Diplomacy, Risk and/or Imperial.  Personally, I find alliances to be sort of limiting.  Maybe it was magnified by us not being able to muster any additional units until Round FIVE!  This game would be more appealing if the first deck was separated into even distributions of Supply/Muster so that the game doesn't stall.    

Anyway, I controlled House of Lannister.  I enjoyed that the Five Houses have variable powers in terms of House Cards.  I also liked the bidding for control of the Throne, Sword and Raven.  The simultaneous action selection of orders was probably my favorite feature.

[b]Storm of Swords[/b]
I really enjoyed playing with the Storm of Swords expansion.  Perhaps it was better playtested!  Anyway, I like the alternate map for 4 players, the new Westeros deck, the inclusion of leaders, the tactic cards, and the ally decks.  Once again I was House of Lannister, but in this version I didn't feel like my faction was inherently screwed by my starting board position.

[b]Clash of Kings[/b]
Played the base map again as a 6-player game with the Clash of Kings expansion.  We used the 6-player map and the Ports, but we didn't use the siege engines/fortifications, special order tokens, or the COK House cards.  This session was a little more enjoyable than my first attempt at AGAT, for we were able to muster up some troops to actually do things, but this time around we were unable to supply until round 7!  Although I would play AGAT with 5 or 6 players again, I still believe that the game would be better if the deck was separated into even distributions of supply/muster.

RATING: 4
After 3 plays, I have to lower my rating on this one from a 6 to a 4.  Navies are just too powerful in this game.  The fact that navies can support several adjacent armies at once, but cannot be counter-attacked by armies (nor can armies support fleets in sea battles) makes thematic sense but is very unbalanced.  Once a power (usually Greyjoy and Baratheon) has fleets in each space of their respective ocean they can daisy chain armies from one side of the continent to the other, making it impossible for non-naval powers to defend more than one coastal territory.  I am amazed at the popularity of this game despite the balance problems between the players.

RATING: 9
The more I play this game (with the Clash of Kings expansion) the more I want to play. The level of intrigue and diplomacy make this one of the best "war" games I have ever played. The game has minimal luck - and usually extremely managable at that. It is all about out-thinking your opponent, something which I am determined to one day finally achieve.

I like the way there are a finite number of 'opening' moves with each house, but the game always runs a different course each time you play. There is a delicate balance between the houses in play, but - as a game of skill - new players will get slaughtered by experienced groups.
If you haven't played this yet, you must get a group together and try it! (more than once, so you can give it the chance it deserves.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A 7 for the mechanics, but the long play time means it won't see the table as often.

I want to love this game, but I find I really dislike absolute number crunching - I'd much rather roll a die, rather than calculate all the numbers required to attack or defend.  I've also learned that I dislike long, 3+ hour multiplayer diplomacy games.  Give me a 2 hour multiplayer conflict game, sure.  Give me a 3+ hour 1v1 wargame, sure.  Give me a 3+ hour 6-player diplomacy game?  I might pick something else.

I want to like it, but once you hit turn 5 or so, there's a lot to analyze, made worse by the fact that everything is 100% deterministic.  Frequently, the game takes 4 hours and someone wins due to someone else not paying attention, or everyone just calls it quits.

IMPORTANT BALANCE SUGGESTIONS:  Do yourself a favor and use these:

You can make the Ports (one of the main issues, just look at the Expansion rules) yourself out of cardboard.  Very easy.  

And Pyke should not exist in a four player game.  Don't play this game with three players.

Finally the last balance change for a great AGOT experience:

You should also play with the "Subdeck" variant, as random Westeros cards are a pain.  The Subdeck variant is as follows:  Put two of each "Event" card (for Decks 1 and 2) in a subdeck.  Subdeck 1 has four event cards (two of each type) and the Last Days of Summer.  Subdeck 2 has the other remaining 5 cards.  (Two of each type, and Winter is Coming.)  Put Subdeck 1 on top.  Things are still slightly random, but each event WILL occur twice before a third comes up.

Lannister could also use an extra footman in the 5 player game, on Lannisport.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played it once at a friends place. We were going to play the second edition with 6 players. Unfortunately the guy who owned the second edition couldn't make it, so we played this first edition with 5 people instead.

A great gaming experience. Now I'm looking very much forward to trying the second edition.

RATING: 4
I don't play many war games, and I don't like run-away leaders and elimination games.  AGOT has a lot of limiting factors that keep people in the game and keep army sizes close, which I thought would improve the game.  But when I played the game, instead of improving it, I felt like it made the game go nowhere.  Armies seemed sluggish.  There was little movement on the board.

I thought having such an important thing as mustering your armies be completely random was lame, and I don't see why resource allotment was even needed as an event.  It really makes little sense why an army can still be taking advantage of resources a few turns to possibly many turns AFTER it has lost them simply because an event hasn't yet randomly occured.  Now, here's the kicker--the random event cards have a reshuffle event in them, so these very important parts of the game really are COMPLETELY RANDOM.

I think this game has potential, but my play of it was just BORING, and the completely random events seem to do nothing to me but remove a big chunk of skill.

RATING: 8
A Game of Thrones is a wonderful game, skillfully carved to recreate the same atmosphere and feeling a reader can find in Martin’s Song of Ice and Fire: actually it has only one weakness, yet this weakness is more than enough to strip the game any fun, delight and wish to play it often.

I’m not talking about the wooden bits (plastic miniatures would have been a better choice), its endless duration or the endless downtime running between performing an action and the next one: no, what I’m referring to is that awful «Risiko syndrome» affecting as much A Game of Thrones as many other strategy games.

Indeed, should a player fall behind the others (and in a crowded five or - with the expansion - six player game it’s more than likely) he’s out of the run for the next of the game, no matter how hard he struggles to recover: he’s already lost and he quickly realizes he’s no longer any chance to win. 
This turns the game into a boring and frustrating waste of time for that unlucky player or two, usually the same ones who agreed on playing the game even though they ain’t too fond of the genre or familiar with its setting.

Nonetheless, the game itself is solid, carefully developed and deeply soaked in theme, which is the stuff of any good game. (December 2011)

[b]Update[/b] - After having played the second edition as well (in January 2013), I found the latter slightly better and more enjoyable than this first edition. Please refer to the [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/103343/a-game-of-thrones-the-board-game-second-edition]second edition[/url]'s entry for my updated comment and rating.

RATING: 2
Looks like a fantastic strategy game for 4+ players.  I'd love to give it a shot.  It's on the way from FFG!

After the first couple of plays I'm intrigued by this game.  At first glance it seems simple, but then the strategy of order placement and learning to anticipate opponent's moves starts to come into play.  I see the card-based mustering as a bit of a weakness, especially with the "Winter is Coming" cards shuffling the used cards back into the Westros decks.  I'd like to play this more.

Three plays, three games where we didn't see a mustering card for the vast majority of the game.  Totally broken, there is no way that a conflict game like this should be able to stick the players with only the starting units for over HALF THE GAME.  We basically sat there calling each other nasty names for over an hour because we could not do much more than that.  I cannot see why people like this game, much less rate it so highly.  Off to the trade pile.

RATING: 6.8
Fuedal mayhem... the houses of old... and squabling amongst the duchies.  

I really want to like game as its theme etc warms my heart.  There is something about its balance that I haven't been quite able to grasp.  The components are nice the board is lovely and the theme sucks you in.  The mechanics are simple and easy to learn.  How to bleeding win is another matter though.  Do not!! get behind on unit growth everything...EVERYTHING in this game is based on having lots of units and the being able to expend those units and still protects your resources from invasions.  

I will play this game but there is something tragically diabolic about how tedious it becomes as far as tactics etc.

Weaker houses absolutely need to form alliances with specific goals of territory and elimination of more powerful houses.(the ones with better consolidate power...(the thronesword and raven special powers are always held by the highest bidder so he who controls that and maintains that will be best at winning.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Based on only one play.  Very similar to Wallenstein in the orders mechanic and development of the game (a good thing IMO).  I give it an advantage over Wallenstein in the nice supply mechanic that frustratingly limits units in each area where in Wallenstein you can stack units until the cows come home.  I also like the support and raid orders.  On the down side, the randomness of the supply and muster cards can lead to some unnecessary stagnation.  I also prefer the luck element in the battle tower compared to the perfect information in AGOT battle system.  Lastly, your starting position in AGOT can be disadvantaged whereas in Wallenstein, starting positions are relatively equal.
Have played four more times, including the new second edition rules.  Second edition fixes the mustering problem.  However, I believe supply should be tallied every round.  Most of the board is a military stalemate thanks to very limited casualties in battles.  Navies are ridiculously overpowered.  The fleet chaining is necessary due to the stalemate on land, but fleets supporting in land battles is a joke.  Also, Greyjoy seems overpowered, winning four of five games I have been in (with different players).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[10/10/05]

Being  a great DIPLOMACY afficionado, I was eager to have a go at AGOT - essentially a rules & map variant of the aforementioned classic.

While I appreciate the sheer elegance, strategic depth and psychological finesse of DIPLOMACY, I am happy to also play a negotiation game which does not rival the game in these respects.

AGOT is not a "purist" Diplomacy variant. That is to say, it adds a good dose of luck. I personally find the means of conflict resolution really interesting. Instead of the deterministic manner of resolution, the cards etc add a nice sort of uncertainty. Both systems work very nicely and I happen to like them both. Here, in AGOT one is to no little degree the "maker of one's own luck". 

However, the luck factor is simply out of control in regards to the Westeros cards. In our game the first auction occurred IIRC in the 8th round. This can entirely screw players - as do other cards not appearing. I believe the game should have allowed for more control and/or predictability.

As a student of Diplomacy variants, I am also wary of the balance of the various powers. I suppose the designer sought to make all powers equal. I fear he's missed the mark.

Further, fleets are given a too dominant role. Certainly, the port rule from the Clash of Kings expansions is a mandatory fix. Evidence of the game lacking in polish and balance out of the box.

I personally would have also included "ocean" spaces, i.e. sea spaces connecting the the sea spaces already present. Three big ones roughly one each for the three maritime sides of the board would have been nice and made the action more fluid and navies less able to seal off whole areas. Furthermore, that would edge powers presently far away from each other, closer together. This would encourage greater interaction (and paranoia). The geopolitics of AGOT would thus be more interesting (if not anywhere to the extent of DIPLOMACY).

Bottom line: A fine game, but not without its issues. Certainly could have done with some more analysis by Diplomacy variant design experts. But then playtesting and polish isn't the forte of Petersen. See Twilight Imperium and it's evolution.

No classic and nowhere close to the grandeur of Diplomacy. But then, hey, few games - if any - measure up to that momentous standard. 

A good game for those "afraid" of Diplomacy. Arguably also shorter. And suits five players.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
GoT is one of my favourite area control/Risk-like games.  Playing actions face-down promotes bluffing, table-talk, and the ability to predict players' actions.  The three influence tracks add variety, and the asymmetry of each player's starting position makes for interesting gameplay.

The combat mechanic of army strength + a card from your hand means that you can lose, despite having a larger force if your card is poor.  Planning when to use your high-value cards and when to use lower-value ones is very important.  Tracking what cards your opponents have used allows you to plan when to attack with the highest probability of success.

The political intrigue of A Song of Ice and Fire is well-realized in this game.  Overall this is a very well-designed game with only a few slight problems:

1- Without harbours (a feature in one of the expansions, later added to the second edition) it is VERY easy for a single boat to cripple an entire player's navy and prevent them from ever rebuilding it.  Baratheon and Greyjoy players frequently park single ships in most sea areas, allowing them to hold the entire coastline uncontested.

2- Infrequent/irregular opportunites to build forces can stagnate the combat element of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Some interesting mechanisms. I like the "bid for influence / reevaluate supply only when an event card comes up for it" aspect, which adds tension to the game.  Seems to be influenced by Diplomacy in some ways -simultaneous orders (but unlike Diplomacy, not simultaneous order resolution); support orders; convoys (though ships don't have to be ordered to convoy, they just have to be there); and the fact that you're never bound by agreements you make. But it's by no means a Diplomacy clone.

RATING: 8
I'm learning that I really like games that are like Diplomacy but that aren't... well... Diplomacy! Gimme a multiplayer game with dudes on a map, area control, etc. and I'm generally happy. So, I was pre-disposed to liking A Game of Thrones, along with Senji, Shogun, and oddly enough Dominant Species. They all have their strengths, and I'll play any of them.

With A Game of Thrones, I'm most intrigued by a very deterministic system for combat. That, more than any of the other titles I mentioned, feels closest to Diplomacy. But I also really enjoy the auctioning off of combat strength, turn order, and order flexibility. Very cool system, and there doesn't seem to be a lot of fluff. My only knock is that the randomness of the event cards can sometimes throw you for a loop, and it's odd to claim a region and have to wait a couple of turns to actually reap its benefits. Still, the decks may make each game unique.

And lastly, wooden bits? No combat dice? Steamlined set of rules? Who'd a thunk that this was a Fantasy Flight game!

As with most Fantasy Flight Games products, it is a great game, but the box is twice the size it needs to be.

RATING: 7.4
[b]General initial comment:[/b]
Need to play again ASAP. Note that the Greyjoy should start at the the lowest position on the initial King's Court track contrary to their House Start Card (and contrary also to the Tyrell's card, these two should be switched--Errata). The ports from ACoK expansion are mandatory. The siege engines/fortifications seem like a good idea for experienced players (have not tried this ... also, am not an experienced player). The alternate rules for the Westeros deck seem very playable, but have not tried them.
[b]Comments on Game Play:[/b]
TBA.
[b]Comparison to Diplomacy:[/b]
I think, that after 1 play, this game has eliminated any desire for me to actually [i]organize[/i] (note, not play) a game of Dilomacy. This game is shorter, has more challenging decisions, doesn't require as much space, has a more impressive (though ignorable) thematic experience, and seems easier to talk people into.
[b]Final notes:[/b]
Suffers from the same whiny little bi*ch complaints that Diplomacy suffers from, so choose players with some care.

All 6 player games will be logged under ACoK

RATING: 6
A Game of Thrones (AGoT) is one of those games which would be a better game if it were played more often. Other games which fall into this category are: Die Macher, Silverton, Fury of Dracula, and Arkham Horror. If some GRAB members could set aside a night for these games once a week we'd enjoy them more. Too many rules, tactics and strategies are forgotten between games. This game improves with each play. Focusing on one enemy at a time seems the best course of action. Do not be fearfully of player elimination. House Stark, which I have played twice, is powerful, but needs to gain supplies as quickly as possible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Update: I think I get worse at this game every time I play. Starting to get annoyed with the combat cards - it's too important that you know exactly what cards an opponent has available in a combat. It bogs down the game, and really kills the surprise involved in playing the cards. I'd prefer some situation where there's more randomness rather than the same decks, or getting rid of any cards with special abilities.

Original comment: A fun game. A great deal of chaos arises from the Westeros cards, and the timing of Supply, Muster, and the Clash of Kings can make planning and strategy difficult, and has perhaps too strong an influence on the outcome. You also have to be really careful about players being allowed to run free for a couple of turns without challenge - if one player is left alone for even a short while, it's very likely they will win. Since all players cannot readily challenge all players if they're already in a struggle elsewhere, alliances are important.

Downside: this game can really get bogged down by slow players. With good players, it's a 3-4 hour game. With slow players, it can go on...and on... and on...

So if these elements sound like positives, you are going to love this game.  If they bother you, you may want to look for a different game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've played this a handful of times.  Can't say I'm honestly a fan of either the game or books it's based off of, but it manages to be a decent experience despite this.  It's all about positioning and deal-making, while planning for what might come out of the event decks.  You have to balance building up your reserves of the game's currency with denying it to your enemies.  Castles are vital spawn points, but you won't get very far without a supply base that will support a large army.  There are a lot of limitations on what you can do, since there are only so many order tokens you can place.  
Because of this, it kind of feels like it takes a lot longer than it should.  But, planning all your orders is tricky, and resolving them in turn order (which fluctuates) isn't quick either.  Even though it's simple, it's also time-consuming.  

My big complaints are the combat and the rich-get-richer problem.  If your neighbors decide to make an alliance and carve up your territory, you pretty much have no chance to stop them.  Maybe if you're lucky, you can secure an alliance with someone else nearby, but if you're back in a corner, that may be too little, too late.  And the other players might well be too busy with their own problems to 'help' you, even opportunistically.  
The combat system of fixed values with secretly committed leaders sounds good, and it can be.  But, if people are being cagey about getting into combat, then it can be very boring.  If you and your opponent are the only ones fighting, and you both have full hands, then you know exactly what he could play.  Now, there's still some bluff here.  You'll get into situations where your only chance to win is to play your highest-power leader.  But, your opponent knows this, so he just has to play his highest to stop you.  But, you could fake him out by playing a low one and losing purposefully, but he knows that, and can destroy your troops if you do..  
I've decided after playing several games like this that I hate deterministic combat.  Won't go into specifically why, but in this game If your opponent is better-positioned &/or has more forces, he can steamroll you and there's nothing you can do about it.  Worse, you can't even cost him anything for stealing your territory, since there's no attrition.  That's what really hurts my enjoyment of the game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I have a love of all things related to the George R.R. Martin book series 'A Song of Ice and Fire', and so I naturally decided to buy this and try it.  I fell in love after the first game and played it many times.  In fact, this is the board game that made me interested in board games as a hobby.  

In game of thrones you have map with various regions which you will try to control, often through conflict with your knights and footmen. In each of these regions, there will be a number of icons which give the controlling player a benefit. The ocean is also segregated like the regions and people will fight naval combat to control these areas since the naval units can assist coastal land battles and increase the mobility of land units.

The most interesting part of the game is when it comes to placing orders for your units (move/attack, get power, support adj regions, defense, raids: destroy power/support commands). All players place orders (card board tokens) face down simultaneously which hides your selected order from your opponents. After everyone has laid all of their orders they are all revealed simultaneously and executed in player order (which will change throughout the game). It's very fun trying to anticipate what opponents will do. Throughout the game you will get power tokens which will allow you to bid on area of influence (player order, combat superiority, and special orders) as well as you can leave these power tokens behind in regions to maintain control without having units there.

Combat is fun in that there is no dice-rolling randomness (I'm scowling at you Risk and WotR). You compare combat values of your units (footman is a value of 1, knights are 2, boats are 1) and your opponents units. These values can be altered by any supporting units in adjacent regions (which could be yours, your opponents, or another players altogether... negotiating and backstabbing makes things interesting) and then each person selects one of their combat cards from their hand of cards which adds combat value and typically a special ability. Losing combat doesn't always mean you will lose units either which is part of the strategy... maybe trying to get opponents to use all of his good cards and then strike decisively.

The game has a set number of rounds. You win by controlling the most regions with city/town icons. If you attain a specific number you can automatically win right there and then. This usually happens in my experience.

Simply a fantastic game based on a fantastic series of books called 'A Song of Ice and Fire' by George R.R. Martin. You in no way need to read any of the books to get enjoyment out of the game.

There are a few gripes about the game, the first is that it's never certain when you're actually going to be able to make new units since at the beginning of every round except the first you resolve 3 random card actions that affect everyone. In the first set of cards there could be: make new units, adj army sizes/capacity based on supply, and bidding on the 3 area of influence. I personally don't mind this but it has agitated others.

The second is that the game truly shines with the maximum number of players: 5. If you have less than that I would recommend getting the Storm of Swords expansion and play that with 4 players, or to play another game entirely. Five players only for the base game is a legitimate complaint.

There are 2 expansions to the game, one that allows a 4 player game on a different board with a lot of new game mechanisms (some are compatible with the base game, some are only for the new board), and another that allows up to 6 players on the original board slightly altered with a map overlay. There are also numerous options in both expansions that are optional.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Game of Thrones takes the simultaneous turn order and lack of randomness found in the territory acquisition classic "Diplomacy" and makes it more enjoyable in some key ways: (1) the game is far shorter (2) it plays well with smaller groups (3) it adds half a dozen simple mechanics (supply, action, card drawing which affects the environment and can add a "barbarian invasion".  This is my favorite territory acquisition game.  In this game fortune favors the bold: "When you play the game of thrones, you win or you die."

RATING: 9
I initally found bgg because I did a search on my (at that time) favorite game "Junta".  I spent time scanning through the games and found one based on my favorite fantasy series - and not only that, it was rated VERY high!  Checked out all the info and put it on my wish list and got it for Christmas - only played it once so far, but I love it!  I can't wait to play it again - it is my new favorite game!

It is a 'balance' game - you need to expand, but you only have so many units to defend your territory and may go for turns without receiving reinforcements.  You are surrounded by enemies, all of which will gladly make an agreement and then betray you the following turn.  You have to worry about gaining supply, power, and cities/strongholds.  Very strategic game.

RATING: 6
This could have been Diplomacy with chrome. The bits were nice, the game was tied to a well fleshed-out storyline and setting, and the artwork was of sufficient standard. But alas, the chrome overwhelmed the diplomacy. Why Diplomacy worked was because the 'clean' game system focused players on fully exploiting the negotiation mechanism. But in AGOT, it was all about the chrome, and worse, the rules created by the chrome was fiddly and unintuitive and added to game imbalance and starting position problems. In Diplomacy, the only real difference between players was in the starting positions but the 'clean' system made sure that that was the only imbalance. Not so in AGOT! I understand that the game was built in some way to resemble the situation in the novels. In that case, then only those who read the novels will understand why the game rules are such and be willing to accept them. For the rest, the game rules will cut no ice. I wouldn't completely write this game off. This game is all about the theme, which can be quite appealing. I would leave the option open of another game only if there was really nothing else better to play (very rare occurance).

RATING: 9
I am still very new to this game, but it continues to grow on me the more I play it.

I love the hidden ordering system, and the tightness of the map and the battle system.  There is a chess-like tension associated with trying to assess what the other players are going to do, and where their strengths and weaknesses are.  The scarcity of troops further adds to the tension, as it becomes difficult to defend one's territory as one continues to expand.

Personally, I like the uncertainty that the Westeros card deck brings, not really knowing when the muster or the supply cards will come, though I respect the complaints about this. 

AGOT is a fabulous game that really shines with 5, or with 6 using the ACOK expansion.

RATING: 8
Excellent components, lots of strategy and nice mechanics.  What prevents it from rating any higher is a nagging feeling that  not all starting position are equally balanced and the kingmaking possibilities.  Will still play it although a word to the wise to not let a player rule the seas (especially not red or black).  Movement and retreat are greatly aided by a strong sea power; if this is uncontested for too long, the game will end sooner.  Also, try to keep strong diplomatic ties with a neighbor as you will sometimes need to fight useless battles just to reset your battle hand.  Especially handy if you're down to that last "0" strength battle card and everyone knows it...

RATING: 9
Does a great job of capturing the feel of the books.  Those unfamiliar with the series can still greatly enjoy the game, but fans will get an extra kick out of all the minor characters on the house cards and so forth.  (The [thing=19400]Storm of Swords[/thing] expansion does an even better job of reinforcing the theme.)  It doesn't hurt that it's a fantastic game system with negotiation, action, and very little luck (other than, of course, the random selection of houses before play).

My only two criticisms: 1) the Ports from the [thing=10873]Clash of Kings[/thing] expansion are necessary and should have been included (note that they are included in the second edition); and 2) holy crap, it's hard to play Lannister. We've adopted the errata which suggests switching Greyjoy's and Tyrell's spots on the King's Court track, which helps out early on, and there are some other expansion elements that help, but it's still extremely difficult.

Rating is for the base game with the Ports. I also prefer to use the alternate House Cards from ACOK, and the alternate Westeros cards and the Wildling deck from ASOS. But all of that is negotiable: at a minimum, I will insist upon using the Ports, as will most experienced players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"Le Trône de Fer" est l'un des premiers jeux modernes auxquels j'ai joué et que j'ai acheté. Je connaissais le "Munchkin" et quelques autres choses depuis quelques années, mais pas de réel jeu de plateau avec une profondeur stratégique. Et puis un jour, un pote qui m'avait, justement, fait découvrir "Munchkin", me propose de participer aux "RLB" (Rencontre Ludiques de Bretagne) en 2009 et là, c'est la plongée dans cet univers merveilleux. Plongée tardive peut-être, alors que beaucoup de choses ont été faites et que j'ai du retard sur ce qui peut se faire dans cet univers du jeu de société, mais plongée en profondeur malgré tout, avec deux journées intensives lors desquelles j'ai pu en partie voir ce qui existait et combler certaines lacunes ludiques. Je ne vais pas faire la liste, mais ce fut l'occasion de découvrir des choses comme "Diplomacy" ou "Les Colons de Catane".

Et en soirée, donc, "Le Trône de Fer", lors d'une partie à cinq joueurs. Ayant joué plus tôt dans la journée à "Diplomacy", le mécanisme d'alliances, trahisons et programmation m'était donc plus ou moins connu et l'assimilation des règles a été rapide. Découverte prolongée d'un achat assez rapide, ainsi que de celui d'autres jeux, avant une pause assez longue par manque de camarades de jeux après un déménagement, et une reprise de plus grosse envergure vers la rentrée scolaire 2011.

Voilà pour ma vie. Ce jeu tient donc, vous l'aurez compris, une place un peu particulière dans ma carrière de joueur. De plus, j'ai découvert l'univers de George R. R. Martin avec le jeu de plateau, ne connaissant ni les livres ni la série télévisée, que je n'ai regardé que tardivement et sans réel intérêt, par ailleurs. En dernière année de licence d'histoire, j'ai ressorti ce jeu qui prenait la poussière, trouvant enfin des joueurs pour m'accompagner dans mes péripéties ludiques. Et vu que c'était un peu le seul jeu de cette catégorie que je possédais, on en a enchaîné pas mal de parties. Et ils ont accroché, en gros. Chaque partie n'avait pas la même saveur, certes, et la configuration optimale reste sans hésiter celle à cinq joueurs (on a essayé toutes les autres), voire à six lorsque l'extension l'a permise, mais dans tous les cas, on passait à chaque fois un bon moment, avec son lot d'alliances foireuses, de déceptions, de fourberies, de trahisons spectaculaires et de retournement de situations.

Car ce qui fait tout le piment du jeu, c'est justement la composante psychologique et humaine de la chose, le doute et l'analyse des autres joueurs, la confiance, la faculté de trahir au bon moment qui permet de faire gagner la partie, mais peut aussi se retourner contre vous et causer la ruine. Bien sûr, et c'est pour ça qu'il n'obtient pas la note maximale, le jeu n'est pas exempt de défauts. Certaines parties voient s'instaurer une forme de stabilité voire d'ennui, où personne ne fait rien de peur des effets néfastes, d'autres au contraire, par un tirage aléatoire et dans un "mauvais ordre" des cartes rendent la position intenable pour un joueur (mais une variante permet de limiter ce phénomène). Enfin, pour moi, le côté aléatoire est aussi ce qui permet de mettre un peu de sel dans le jeu, j'aime ne pas maîtriser absolument tout mais devoir composer avec ce qu'on m'impose, et je n'ai jamais eu l'impression que ça ruinait ma partie.

Le défaut de l'immobilisme, lui, se traduit par les joueurs présents autour de la table et la question du tempo, on reste parfois dans l'attentisme quelques tours, à jauger ses adversaires, puis on tente un coup, on se dit qu'on s'emmerde et que comme ça on ne gagnera jamais, donc autant y aller. Parfois ça paye, d'autres fois il fallait le faire plus tôt, ou plus tard, et ça ne marche pas. Mais c'est là toute la subtilité du jeu, ce qui fait tout son intérêt, au-delà de toutes les mécaniques qui le compose (enchères, cartes à jouer lors de batailles...), qu'il ne faut tout de même pas négliger sans quoi la meilleure stratégie du monde aura du mal à atteindre son but.

Alors oui, il y a la question de l'équilibre des positions de départ, des cartes des différentes maisons, de tout ce que vous voulez. Mais ce jeu est-il fait pour être absolument équilibré ? Je pense le contraire. On commence à un endroit, on en sait les caractéristiques, les avantages, les difficultés, chaque joueur en fera ce qu'il voudra, essaiera de dépasser ce déséquilibre de départ à sa façon et élaborera de nouvelles stratégies, de nouvelles alliances, afin de composer avec ce déséquilibre. Parce que les jeux super bien équilibrés, c'est très bien, mais lutter contre le déséquilibre d'un jeu fait aussi partie du jeu, quand cela est possible. Et je pense que dans "Le Trône de Fer", ça l'est, et que la façon de le faire pour essayer de gagner, en sachant s'adapter tant à un déséquilibre qu'au hasard, la complémentarité à trouver pour s'équilibrer et prendre le pouvoir, est l'une des forces de ce jeu.

Un très bel objet ludique, à sortir avec précaution toutefois, qui ne conviendra pas à tous, long et qui n'est pas dénué de défauts ou de critiques potentielles, mais qui garde une place particulière dans ma ludothèque. D'une part par forme de nostalgie, mais surtout (la nostalgie c'est plus tellement mon truc) parce que c'est, tout simplement, un très bon jeu.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Received in trade 02/21/06


Fantastic game of diplomacy and warfare.

EDIT:  One of my favorite games of all time.  Really creates some tense battle situations...the map starts out looking like there's room for everyone, but two turns in, everyone is in each other's face and knives and blades are drawn.

Look, I respect Diplomacy, but there is no reason to play that in a world where this game exists.  Reason being, there's an actual game here that can exist independently of the player's bitching and dealing with each other.  Dip is a game pushed solely along by the players with a bare bones rules structure.  This is a game where you have that player interaction, but an actual set of meaty rules. 

Also, you can actually get things done on your own in this game, which is nice.

Faithful rendering of the battles in the books, and one of the best times that five or six like-minded players are going to have.



ALSO OWN:

A Clash of Kings (1st edition)  https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgameexpansion/10873/game-thrones-clash-kings-expansion
A Storm of Swords (1st edition)  https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgameexpansion/19400/game-thrones-storm-swords-expansion

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[i]"When you play the game of thrones, you win or you die. There is no middle ground."[/i] --Cersei Lannister describing how this game feels to play.

[b][i]Game of Thrones[/i][/b] is not a relaxing game. You will agonize over order placement while hoping you outthought your bitter rival only to be stabbed in the back by your staunchest supporter. There's little hidden information, so the game feels very cerebral. For example, suppose I'm ahead in a battle: I'm guaranteed a victory if I play (and thereby "spend") my good card. But my opponent knows this and will likely not play his good card meaning I could still win if I play a bad card. But what if he suspects I might try to save my good card and then uses his good card on the off-chance that I play loosely thereby stealing a win... you get the idea. This game is a blast to play.

[b]First Edition vs Second Edition[/b]
The 2nd edition incorporated the best parts of the expansions from the 1st edition and is, to my eyes, clearly superior.

RATING: 10
Well I always believe in using the full scale in rating systems, rather than holding out for a mythical 'perfect' game...

I just really adore the combination of the order placement system and the track/bidding mechanism. Great deep strategy and drama, fun both face-to-face and PBEM (see Yahoo group). Plus the tactical complexity is surprisingly rich - I spent upwards of an hour mulling over some positions in a PBEM game, only to finally realize there was precisely one sound defensive scheme that would handle all permutations of threats - a satisfying insight.

Sure it's imperfect, but the only thing that actually intrudes on my fun (and only slightly) is the inability to challenge at sea once you're pushed off it, but the ports in the expansion fixes this fine. Even without that I'm happy playing it - you just have to know the stakes at sea are high going into it.

Plus it's based on a great book series (basically crack for gamer types.) I heartily encourage you to take a chance on this one - buy the book and the game together for a great gift package. :)

RATING: 6
I have heard that the game has some balance issues, but it wasn’t apparent on our first game. After playing it a few times I can definitely see them. The first games we got some rules wrong, but through repeated plays I think we have straightened all out.

The game itself is really good. I like the bluffing aspect with putting out the orders in the game and I think it is the main tactical decision in the game. There is also some resource management, but it is hard to plan for that.

I can see now where StarCraft has got their inspiration from and I personally like this more as the game play flows more. This feels more pure, but StarCraft has an interesting combined arms aspect. I can also see that this game got some inspiration from games like diplomacy although it plays more like Britannia. I expect this to stand a lot of plays and I will probably upgrade the rating with time.

RATING: 7
One of the better Fantasy Flight Games (I own TI and WoW as well). This one gets a lot of points for an innovative (to me at least) combat system which often adds a great deal of strategy and opponent-guessing despite being basically a simple matter of addition. As well, the bidding for the various positions available in the game makes for some of the most nerve-wracking and fun moments I've ever had playing a game.

The standardized setup that results in few, if any, options for opening moves, the unbalanced nature of the factions, and the inevitable King-Making (not necessarily a bad thing, I enjoy a social or revenge element to winning a game) count against it.

RATING: 6
A decent area control game. I like the auctions for control of the three tracks and the combat system is quite clever, incorporating a similar mechanic to LOTR: The Confrontation where cards are played and added to the combat strength of each army.

After two games I do think it has a few issues regarding the variable powers of each house, both in terms of map position and combat card powers. The aforementioned auctions are clever but they require the use of power tokens which are harder to come by for some houses than others and can therefore cause unbalances. This may all be trying to make the game follow the books in some way (I haven't read them) but in terms of gameplay I think it can make it slightly unbalanced.
The drawing of event cards each turn can also make the game swing wildly different ways depending on what comes out and when.

Overall I do like it but I feel that if I was going to play this kind of area control game I'd be more likely to play Struggle of Empires as I don't think it has the same sort of imbalance issues and also scales better for different numbers of players.

RATING: 8
Similar in feel with Diplomacy, but without the written orders. There are several significant and innovative game mechanisms here: 1) Players use command tokens to secretly commit orders to their pieces. The players all reveal orders simultaneously and resolve orders per turn order; 2) Turn order, combat strength, and number of additional 'special' orders are determined by three hierarchy tracks, where the order (or ranking) is literally 'bought' by blindly bidding political power tokens, which are accumulated during the game like income; 3) Combat is simple: total up combat strength of both sides, based on the number and types of units involved. But there is a twist: each player has a set of seven combat cards which can modify their strength or sabotage the other side, dramatically altering the nature of the battle. Once used, the card is set aside until all seven cards are used, or refreshed by some other action. There's lots going on, but it's deep with strategy, tactics, politics, intrigue, and theme. It definitely needs five to play, or six with the expansion, and will take 3-4 hours, with about 45-60 minutes to explain the rules. It's a brain burner, with complex mechanisms, so if one is not familiar with Diplomacy, it will be a rare event if it hits the table.

Update (05/29/11): A rare event that four others wanted to play this after seeing the cable show and/or reading the book. I have done neither, but still enjoyed the game immensely. Plenty of politics and strategic maneuvering abound, makes this game one hell of an immersive adventure. Unfortunately, with several newbies, this 5-player game took 6.5 hours. Perhaps with practice, we can get this down to a more manageable period. Still, a great game, and although with a seemingly overwhelming number of simple mechanics, everything work perfectly and seamlessly. I was surprised to see that FFG put out a 2nd edition. Based on some cursory looks, the graphics have taken a more medieval, middle-earth look, less colorful, and more dreary, more intense re-imaging.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Rating after first play. 

Played AGoT for the 1st time yesterday, with the 6 player extension "Clash of Kings".

At first everything is a bit confusing and you really don't know what you're supposed to be doing. Formed an alliance with 3 other players, more out of their own initiative than mine, but in the end it almost paid off.

Speaking of the end, only by turns 7-8 did I get into the game and started enjoying it. Game flow is uncomplicated and surprisingly elegant. The haphazard alliances and politics work and you can break them at any time and stab anyone on the back. 

The game has, however, a strong gang-on-the-leader tendency, which in itself suits the theme and gameplay. Bold "Risk-type" wave-conquering is possible but not advisable, especially at the end, where a consolidated realm will prove more efficient against predatory conquerors than joining the merry-go-round of you take this and I take that.

RATING: 7
An interesting attempt at Euro-ising Diplomacy, that somehow doesn't quite seem to be all there. The starting position for Greyjoy/Lannister is much weaker than that of the others (a point acknowledged in the FAQ) so they need to ally from the start, otherwise expect whichever of Baratheon/Stark/Tyrell is least interfered with to win. The victory condition of 7 cities is quite a low bar and the game is entirely dependent on the players to attack the leader otherwise the game will end at mid term, just as it's getting interesting.
The bottom line is that this isn't fun enough to be a 3.5 hour game, there are small map Diplomacy variants that offer much more tension.

RATING: 6.5
The mechanics are very interesting, especially combat/maneuver (order tokens and house cards). The game is great in that it creates complex gameplay through a very clear and not overwhelming set of rules. 

Despite all that it is just not my cup of tea:
- There are no catch up mechanisms besides "bash the leader".
- If you're in a bad position because you made a wrong decision early on, your game is over and you basically just watch the others play until the end.
- The theme could be just anything and is rather boring (I didn't read the books).
- Asymmetric balance of player factions.
- You do not interact with everyone. The map will never get you into conflict with half of the other players.
- I found waiting during the other players' turns boring and too long. Especially since half of the players' actions didn't affect me (because of the map).

I understand the game's appeal but would rather play Small World (shorter and more forgiving), Civilization (more epic, more things to do) or El Grande (more balanced).

RATING: 7
This game could be phenomenal (key word: could).  As it is now, the asymmetrical make up of the kingdoms and board, which could be very clever, ends up highly limiting a player.  If you're Lannister - then it's try to expand without making enemies, Tyrell - take over the south and pray for enough musters to send an army northward, Greyjoy - take to the seas while pushing inland, and so on.  I don't like the bottlenecking you are forced to do based on your geography and initial track positions.  Everything else is top notch.  The components are great, the command mechanic is great, the different influence tracks are great, the battles with cards and supports are great...you name it.  I so want this game to be more than it is, but I guess I'll have to settle for giving it a go every once in a good while...A shame.

RATING: 9
Great game but not without a few problems. The greatness lies in the well-balanced structure of the game, the tension of deploying orders when there are so many variables to consider, the role of informal diplomacy and often tacit alliances, and the shifts in power which can create key opportunities to win the game. The issues marring the game include the asymmetry of the starting positions, the luck of the Westeros cards (combined, these two factors can cause a player to be left out of the running in the early part of the game), the length of the game (when all 10 rounds are played), and the fact that the balance of the game is impaired with 5 players.

RATING: 9
Wow, a wargame with little luck and German-style components... what more could you ask for?  The non-random battles are resolved quickly which is a plus, since in most dice-fest games (A&A and AoM for example) the battles tend to drag on and on.  I like the way that army sizes are limited depending on the amount of supply you have; this prevents players from creating an impossibly large army that sweeps across the continent nuking the weaker players, making the game more balanced.  The supply also makes the game less "fiddly", as you no longer have to wonder if your 15 tanks can take out the 30 infantry, etc.  The only problem I see is the balance between the different houses. House Tyrell (sp?) IMO is very underpowered for example.  It's a fun game even if you've never read the books, but I did read the first one since picking up the game.  Rating is a 9 after playing a 5-player game; a 4-player game receives an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Very interesting and intriguing game of Diplomacy where going into the next room with an opponent to 'discuss' plans and possible alliances is part of the game.

Quite an involved system that I think the CoK exp improves upon and also adds a 6th player. I liked the silent auction that uses your power influence tokens while winning determined where you would be situated on the 'power' track. The combat with cards was good too but I think the event system could use tweaking, especially concerning Mustering although this could lengthen the game.

The game can be cut-throat brutal and almost eliminate someone due to the mustering being left up to the events so be warned not for 'softies'.

7.5 => 4 I've realized that this 1st edition was very unbalanced in many regards, like mustering, ports, Lannister House, maps for 4 or 5 players, etc to the point where you NEEDED the expansions, hence my rating drop here for just the base game (typical old FFG, initially I did not notice some of this due to playing with CoK in my first play). Also, a player could easily be put out of the game early on, either completely eliminated (which is actually preferable sadly) or to the point where they will never catch up as system had nothing built in for it, and either way, this is just not fun for that/those person(s) especially in such a long game

This was also the very first "out of room" negotiation game I ever played way back then, and was a big reason why I liked it as much as I anticipated. Since then I have played Dune & Virgin Queen, and besides the theme both of these games seemed better mechanically (again especially if you are talking about AGOT with no exps) With both expansions this might be an 8 or more for me, but that would also likely assume things not fixed by the expansions are tweaked.

RATING: 8
   Game of Thrones is really an excellent game reminiscent of Diplomacy in many ways.  I've only had a chance to play the game once [multiple times now], but man oh man, what a game it was!  I was tense right up to the bitter end.  On the very last turn I was rejoicing when I knew I had it won, and then crushed and bitter when I realized that my brother was actually going to win, and then jubilant when an unexpected move by my sister actually caused me to win by a tiebreaker!!  

   Not many games have this level of tight gameplay, fierce warring, and relatively short time commitment (given what type of game it is) while still leaving everyone happy and in the game at the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Fantasy wargame set in George R. R. Martin's world of Westeros. Players represent noble houses battling for hegemony of the continent following the death of the king. The game's components are absolutely beautiful. Gameplay is basically "advanced Diplomacy", including simultaneous revealing of units' orders (one of these is the ability to "support" your own or other players' units in adjacent areas), but adding several other clever game mechanisms such as supply, mustering, random events and an abstract (but influential) "power ranking" sub-game. One play-balance issue has been corrected with minimal errata, and other potential issues are being discussed by the game's fans. Looks very promising.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Acquired 2009-06-13

I like everything about that game - except playing it (and explaining the rules). It's a eurofied Diplomacy, with a shorter playing time and more complex rules. The clash of kings is perhaps the best use of the auction mechanic I've seen, and the invasions are a great addition.

Now, it's possible that I just didn't get lucky with the cards (my last two games were marked by no mustering before, I think, the 6th and 7th turns), and it's also possible that playing with beginners that just wanted to attack me more than to win made me like this game a whole lot less. Because, well, that's happened. On paper, it looks like the best game ever, but in the facts... well... not so much. I'll give it more tries, for sure, though!

RATING: 6
A 'Wallenstein' wannabe that does not live up to all of its hype.

Fixed and, from what I've seen, unbalanced starting positions, virtually no randomness in battle, too much randomness in the event cards, and, basically, too much "chrome" for what the game is trying to do.

Even with a 10 turn limit, the game can literally take days to play!  Diplomatic deals alone can eat up three or more  hours of game time.  Each of our games so far has run well over 5 hours and only because of a couple of mistakes made by one of the players... otherwise we'd still be playing!

To have a win 'fall into your lap' because of another's inept play is only a hollow victory at best... too many problems coupled with a high price tag only rate this game a 5.

I really try to find some good in almost every game I play, but this one left a bad taste in my mouth... I might have rated it even lower, but the physical appearance and components make up for the difference.

After only two plays, I strongly doubt that I will ever play this one again.

Addendum:
I managed to play this with 3 players.  With virtually no diplomacy to eat up the clock, the game played in about 100 minutes.  I doubt that I'll ever play this with more players ever again.

RATING: 7
Game of Thrones is my first diplomatic wargame. I love the books and series, so the theme has some additional value for me. But it is also a very good game. I like the fact that secrecy and bluffing play a major part in playing out your strategy. Units are sparse, so making alliances is a necessity to have a chance to win. The base game is best with 5 players, and unbalanced with less. The Storm of Swords expansion makes it a more flexible game, because it becomes very playable with 4 players, and adds some great aspects to the game, such as leaders and executions. This game is high on interaction, and I immensely enjoy revealing the command tokens, and figuring out who has been outbluffing or outmaneuvering whom.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The inspiration by Diplomacy is visible in the basic orders move/defend(hold)/support. To make it more playable the main deterrent to many players, negotiation, has been dimmed down. The game length is thus reduced. To further increase the appeal, a popular fantasy theme was crafted on. To make it work without negotiations, the increased number of units and hand management of battle cards puts more control into each player's hand instead of control solely as a function of his negotiation skills. The end result is a game more akin to usual multiplayer conflict games than Diplomacy. If you like these, you have a good one at hand that is not so much a free-for-all bash the leader fest as many other games in this genre. Purportedly, the balance of the asymmetric player powers is off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Impressions after first game: Diplomacy derivative with auctions for special abilities, card-dictated sequencing of upkeep and other game events, and various small strands of tinsel, but largely without the action cards and accumulated perks that make a monstrosity like TI3. Reasonable adherence to theme given the type of game it is. Would play again.

There wasn't much in the way of overt deals or alliances, more like stretches of unarticulated détente punctuated at the first show of weakness. This may not have been a bad thing, though you lose part of the Diplomacy legacy: negotiation isn't quite the same thing when the rules don't order you to get down to it.

Playing time had better go down upon repeated play: I'm not terribly keen on having a six-hour game decided (in part or in full) by one of the players out of contention making a doubtful attack because he "just wants to win a last fight", no matter how legitimate a decision this is.

RATING: 6
I really want to like this game more than I do. It's got everything I like--area control, card-based combat, negotiation and backstabbing, and friggin Westeros, man, friggin Westeros!!

But it lacks something that similar games I love (Dune, Shogun/Wallenstein, even Mare Nostrum) have: strategical control.  The Westeros cards have too heavy a hand in how each game progresses, and they're determined randomly, without any ability to plan for them in advance. Now, normally I don't mind the tension that this kind of randomness invites. But in this case it makes the game seem oddly pointless.... I think because I expect a certain amount of strategery from a multi-player war game like this, and so having to constantly press the reset button because of the stupid Westeros cards just sucks: shuffle the deck, bid for power, bid again, wildlings attack, no mustering, no mustering, no mustering, shuffle the deck...ahhhhh! That sucks.

Add to this the potential for (and usually the requirement of) player elimination and AGoT falls awfully short of its predecessors and its betters.

RATING: 8
After playing quite a bunch of games and reading the books, I must say this one keeps growing on me. My opinion of it has varied a lot: I started loving it, then I didn't wanted to get close to it, then I rekindled my interest.

Though I did had my doubts, I've come to realize the game's mechanisms work like charm. There is not a single one which could be argued its unnecessary: all help balancing the game in some way or another. 

I like how the only source of "luck" the game has are the event decks. Events force players to rethink their strategies, adapting them when necessary. I've found most players who dislike the game gamble against the event decks, creating strategies which depend of a certain event popping up at a certain time. Wrong: strategies must be carried keeping in mind map distribution, assuming events will hamper you in the worst way possible, while simultaneously being prepared to exploit beneficial events as much as possible the moment they pop up. Events add a dose of chaos and uncertainty, forcing players to adapt their plans. Gambling against the event decks should only be done in the most desperate situations.

I should also mention the game can easily be played "wrong": players need to take risks. If its a single player, its tolerable: excessively defensive strategies are predictable and can be easily outmanoeuvred. If all players are too cautious, the game's pace shatters into pieces, being brought almost to a halt, to the point the game itself becomes unbearably boring. Excessively defensive strategies not only aren't a winning strategies, they can break the game too. Remember I mentioned there was a time I thought I despised the game? It was because of this.

If you want a game which rewards both long term thinking as well as adaptation, all surrounded by an atmosphere of temporary alliances and backstabs, Game of Thrones is for you. Being familiar with the books or the TV series is a huge plus, but not required.

RATING: 8
Tense and fun light wargame which uses simulataneous action chit placement to give it something of the feel of Diplomacy.  The bidding for influence charts shows a debt to Eurogames as well as the game's most obvious strain of forebears.  I also happen to be a fan of the books so the theme works very well for me.  Very nice incorporation of theme and mechanics into an excellent game.  It does seem a bit hard for those who start poorly to recover, however.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A friend of mine brought to our club for viewing and -- playing, how pleasure it was!  A good cross breed between military historical game and Euro-game.  Fantastic mapboard, multiplayers, simple rules, and several beers and, off you and your friends go!  Adventure lies ahead and battles ensue in a yet-to-believe world awaiting for your explore. Suprisingly, it has no gamey strategy or tactics.  

Its basic game "engine" is very solid and has many similarities to war boardgame.  Different houses have their own agendas and ambitions in different stages of the war as their positions or spheres of control shifting and changing on the map.  

I particularly like the initial planning operations for each area with a force where a token has to be placed.  The operation tokens are revealed simultaneously once everyone has made their decisions, you therefore never know what your opponents are up to.  Your plan to attack may, for example, be foiled by a defence order taken by the defender with naval “bombardment” support from neighbouring sea fleets.  Very tense game throughout with this innovative hidden order system, which still shines today more than 10 years after its original release.  

Overall, a marvelous design and a very good game with colourful maps and components, and a lot of possible strategic options throughout the entire 10 years (turns) of the game.  It doesn't get a perfect score of 10 just becuase it is a fantasy game, of which no benchmark you can measure against for its simulation value.  Otherwise, it depicted very well the "true" picture and general "history" of the novel and the land of Westeros.

Nope, this game doesn't do it for me.
