RATING: 3

I really wanted to like this game, however the game play left me flat. 

There seemed to be wild swings in fortune based not at all on strategy, but on luck. I was hoping for a more balanced combat system like diplomacy, however I found that it was very hard to support your own units, since wasting those order meant that you were either not expanding or you were not earning enough power. 
I also found that other than in the most obvious places having another power support you was not going to happen either.  

Sea power (or the movement it provides) is way too strong. 

This game draws on a lot of elements from other games, but I feel they just jumble this game up a bit. The nice balanced combat of diplomcay is ruined by the house cards. The bidding for the 3 power tracks turned into bidding for the raven as the other 2 are far less powerful. 

Also the Map does not feel as balanced as some other games. There are not enough citys on the map. So every turn there is someone who can win. so all alliances shift to stop them, next turn someone else is in that position. Repeat until turn ten when the game ends itself. 

There are many better ways to spend 3-5 hours of your time and money. 

For the cost of this game and the expansion you can go on ebay and buy a copies of AH Dune and Diplomacy. (and have some cash left over) Both of those games do what this one tries to do but much much better. 

Update: 

I tried it again and it still sucks. This time i think it is the perfect information that ruins this game for me. Again the diplomacy was predictable at best and non-existent usually, so battles cam down to players looking at the board and calculating where they could win after the orders were revealed. The playing of the leader cards was a forgone conclusion. Where is the fucking drama of the books? where is the risk? 

this game is just boring.

RATING: 8

RATING: 9
If the game is half as good as the books, it will be awesome. I've got the expansion and I am working on my game group to try this one. Ive got a couple of the guys to read "A Game of Thrones" so the interest in playing is starting to rise. I'd give it a 7 just from reading the rules.

UPDATE: Finally got 5 in the group to play this and it was a huge hit. Took some time to learn the game, but after a few turns the strategies started popping up. we called it at Turn 6 due to time, but everyone wants another go at this one - as well as the 4 that didnt get to play!

Lots of Theme in this. Only a couple of us had read the books, but certain things happened in the game that mirrored events in the book and the rallying cry of the Starks was shouted repeatedly! This game has been asked to be played over the past 3 weeks since we first played it. We had two sets going one night and both games went the full 10 turns. 

The rules to this one are a nice blend of mechanics from games like Diplomacy (Giving orders - but it is handled MUCH simpler here), Cosmic Encounter (Each house has certain abilities through the cards they start with AND the combat system is VERY similar to Cosmic Encounter - add up the tokens, call for allies, play a card, high total wins), and Axis and Allies (Different units with different strengths and abilities).

I really like the Westeros cards and the way they drive the flow of the game between mustering, supply, bidding for the 3 areas of control/power and the Wildling attacks. A Game of Thrones lives up to the novels brilliantly, blending intrigue, combat, diplomacy and resource management in a tight, well executed game that will leave you wanting to play again immediately.

This one is on the verge of being a 10.

RATING: 8
Довольно сложные правила. Объяснять все нюансы минут 30. Но при всем объеме, правила довольно логичны и последовательны. Локализация отличная, ошибок и опечаток не обнаружено. Основная суть игры - создание коалиций, постоянные переговоры и интриги. Рандома не содержит. Возможно некоторые дома не сбалансированы. Игра хоть и не быстрая, но стоит потраченного времени, так как насыщена событиями игровыми. За 2-3 часа, ты можешь заключить выгодный союз, но твой союзник может тебя продать, все ополчаются на тебя - казалось бы 4ро против одного - решено. Но найдя аргумент уговариваешь противника стать надежным союзником и путем общих усилий: кровью и потом наказываешь предателя. Вырезаешь все упоминания о нем с карты... Есть что обсуждать после игры, много интересных стратегий и тактик может отыгрываться, держит в постоянном напряжении.

RATING: 9
Excellent and well designed war game. Quick learning curve and fairly balanced. Nice theme and plays quickly for this type of game!
Variants to use next game:
Setup
Consider giving Lannister (Red) King's Landing with a weak force (1 soldier). Add 1 ship to Green (Tyrell). Add 1 soldier to white (Stark)


Use ports, max 1 sea support, houseruled forts, seige engines.

Base Game Map
Throne can pass on first action
Sword can be used for +1 on anyone after a battle.
Raven can see future, switch commands, and see someone's secret cards.

Start with 4 battle cards each (1 mulligan). Card are public information but you can always choose to hide ONE.

Gain a new card every time a card is used. At the end of your turn, you may discard and gain a new one.

A Castle means you can only lose units caused by swords and it still cancels out a sword.

RATING: 8
He manifestado en muchos sitios mi predilección por este juego. Para mí, es la joya de mi colección. Su mezcla estratégica al más puro estilo del Risk, el alto nivel de interacción de alianzas y vendettas entre los jugadores que puede llegar a dar de sí y la poca influencia que puede ejercer la suerte hace, a mi parecer, que Juego de Tronos sea el juego de los juegos. El juego tiene bastantes aspectos a tener en cuenta sin hacerlo excesivamente pesado o sesudo. Obliga a llegar a acuerdos o dialogar con otros jugadores si se quiere avanzar en el objetivo. Además ofrece momentos de tensión y risas cuando, por ejemplo, surgen los choques de reyes para pujar por las mejores posiciones en las 3 areas de influencia, o cuando los Salvajes atacan. Pero claro, no todo el monte es orégano. Alguna casa está bastante descompensada respecto a las demás (como por ejemplo, la casa negra y amarilla) en cuanto a la brutalidad de sus cartas y/o ventaja en la salida inicial. De hecho, la isla desde donde sale el jugador negro (sólo en partidas de 5 jugadores), es un auténtico fortín muy dificil de conquistar a no ser que el jugador negro haga el cafre. Pero aún a sabiendas de esto, el juego sigue siendo de lo mejorcito a lo que he jugado nunca. Ya se verá si algún día se publica en castellano alguna de las 2 expansiones que el juego tiene en el mercado norteamericano, que entre otras cosas incluye modificaciones en el tablero, 1 nuevo jugador, y añade nuevas reglas y elementos como las caravanas, las fortificaciones y las armas de asedio.

RATING: 9
I have patiently waited for AGOT to get back in stock... in the meantime I stumbled across the book at a used book store, and my oh my, one of the best fantasy books of all time.  I really was now anticipating this game and finally got it.
Clear and conscise rules, great map, great components.  Game play was fantastic!  I would have rated this a 10, but for the lack of ports, which can severly cripple certain players.  I played all my games with the first expansion, fixing the port problem.  The order system, with the ranking of the houses on the three tracks is brilliant, and very engaging.  Combat cards add a nice element of suspense (we play were you can't look at discards or discuss hands, unless you play a combat card that lets you do so)  GRR Martin can be proud of the quality and fun factor for AGOT

RATING: 3
Played once.  My first game of this took approximately five hours to play (including rules), and I don't know if it was the sub-par rules explanation, the extraordinarily slow play, the fact that we had six players, or the constant references to events in the books that went right over my head (I've never read the books), but this was just about the slowest and least interesting five hours of my recent gaming life.  While the basic mechanics of the game seem OK (take over territories to get resources to allow you to build armies to take over more territories), there's enough randomness (e.g., the ability to build armies on any given turn is completely luck-dependent), chaos (each player chooses a card from their hand to support their troops in battle; these cards are unique to each player and have so many special abilities that attempting to predict victory in a battle is nearly impossible), kingmaking (deals between players are encouraged, and ties for some game elements are broken by one person based solely on their whim, not a rule), and unfair start positioning (black's ability to build a strong navy quickly and then control an entire portion of the coast seems unbalanced) to make the game not at all worth the four or five hours it took to play.  

The game has a tremendous volume of rules to go along with it, and while I'm fine with lots of rules, having a player aid or some sort of rules summary would have drastically aided gameplay.  Even after an eternity of rules explanation I and others at my table were still confused about what certain items did and where things could be played, and the winner of our game was decided not by strategy but by a player misremembering a few minor rules (and thus planning their final turn incorrectly).  It was not the type of ending you want after five hours.  

I also found the theme to be entirely uninteresting (probably because I've never read the books), and wonder how much of people's enjoyment of this game is based on the fact that they enjoyed the books rather then that they enjoy the mechanics (as games like Wallenstein, Shogun, Age of Steam, and El Grande have this game beat by far in terms of balanced gameplay).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Use your armies to expand your lands, armies and power to control most victory areas in Westeros.

Plays: A couple.
Type: Thematic Wargame = a theme-driven game where you move military forces on a map to destroy other military forces and/or conquer territory. Area movement style. Strategic scale.
Complexity: Medium.
# of players: 5.
Playing time: 300 mins.
Player interaction: Conflict and Diplomacy.
Randomness: Low – card draw, but cards have board effects that you can prepare for.

Replayability: Very high. The assymetrical starting positions just beg for repeated plays to explore the strategies of the different houses. Every game will be different depending on the diplomatic balance. There is also depth to explore.
Fun: I’m a big fan of multi-player wargames in general and of simultaneous order selection in particular, and this is a home run for me. So much fun with diplomacy and backstabbing, and the perfect theme to go with such a game. Even more fun now that more people know the backstory from the TV-series.
Memorability: Very high. You’ll definitely remember the alliances made and broken and the shifting fortunes of war. The theme shines here as well, making it sound like you’re part of the novels (or the TV-series) when you regale your friends with the tale of your latest victory.

Thematic integration: Very high.
Components: Very good. The map of Westeros is gorgeous and the pieces and chits are of good quality with good art.

Who will like this: Gamers that want a multi-player wargame need look no further. The theme is icing on the cake for those that like it, but you don’t need to to enjoy the game.

RATING: 9
Intense, complex, and full of conflict-based player interaction. Having played this game several more times, I still absolutely love it, but I feel that, like Diplomacy, your starting House determines a lot of your overall success in the game (Lannister for example pretty much gets the shaft, while Stark can usually dominate the north and then sweep down near the end of the game for the win.) These issues are essentially fixed in the Clash of Kings expansion, making it a must, even if only playing a five-player game.

This is yet another game rated highly by the teenagers in the gaming club. Although it's a bit rough explaining all the rules, etc, once they understand how everything works, the game chugs along quickly, and we can usually finish in two or three hours. Some kids are intimidated by the high degree of conflict, but there are enough kids in the club overall that it's not an issue.

You can't have any long-term strategy or planning due to some random Westros card which will ruin all your plans and punish you for planning at all. I am truly, truly, truly and deeply mystified at this game's popularity, especially among my own friends.  Two games in and I'm totally bewildered.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
An interesting twist on Diplomacy. The order tokens are the core design feature, trading off the set in stone quality of orders (since tokens don't reveal the target of attacks/supports) for a much quicker planning phase. Most the others parts, however, feel very FFG: modularity for the sake of it that adds little actual gameplay value compared to how much it makes the game longer and fiddlier. Combat cards are swingy and worth at least half an army by themselves (and were probably only implemented in order to shoehorn book characters into the box), the differing supply/gold of each regions mostly provides us with accounting, random events cards do random things, etc. Plus I don't trust FFG to have put a lot of care in the factions' balance. Amongst those additions I quite like, however, the election phase; possibly because blind bidding is very low on the rules department but very high on the players involvement one. Which is not enough to make me overlook all the ameritrash stuff, but is nice since it gives far apart players a layer of interaction.

If you want to play A Game of Thrones, please note that the base game becomes pretty much broken once a player establishes naval domination on a part of the map; ask people for the harbour rules of the first expansion. (By the way if you're wondering, the rulebook is as messy as usual for FFG.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Based on George Martin's novels, players simultaneously put action tokens on their units to 1)move/attack, 2) support, 3) earn money, 4) raid, 5) defend better.  Attacks are modified from a player's set of cards, in which one has to manage since a card played can only be replenished when the deck is reshuffled, after all cards have been played.  I have only played a 4 player game, but the game seems to be designed for exactly 5 players.

RATING: 7
While not the strongest of Fantasy Flight's more epic scale games, Game of Thrones is an extremely fun game. It suffers somewhat from pacing and the mechanics intertwining with the theme have areas that in retrospect could be stronger. More lavish components such as post 2005 FFG games may also have helped. However, you're still left with a game that very thematically uses diplomacy and simultaneous action selection--one of the best and most underused mechanics in large scale games. I wouldn't always be in the mood to play this game over other war/conquest games but I often would. A strong entry all around.

RATING: 2
I'm really not a fan of this game. First, your ability to build and increase your army size is completely dependent on random card draws. Without knowing when you can get more armies, you have no idea how valuable your few starting armies are. This uncertainty almost inevitably leads to stagnation in the game as nobody wants to take the risk of attacking and everyone just ends up defending their areas until the random supply or mustering comes along. The need to constantly negotiate and plot with and against the other players just isn't fun for me. I prefer to be able to strengthen my own position without having to con other players into helping me out. While I enjoy the hidden orders mechanic, the rest of the negatives keep me away from this game.

RATING: 4.5
A bit too long for what is otherwise a good game. Nice mechanics but a steep learning curve. Some AP, but a good Waro to play every now and then.

Edit: Mar 2010:

I think Starcraft is just outright better. Play that instead. The only reason to play AGOT over SC is the book theme. But theme does not matter to me, and I have not read the books.

What do I not like:

* The mustering (Troop production) is random. The last game I played there were only 2 mustering cards all game, both of which were early on (about turn 2/3 and 4/5). I got a slight territory lead in the mid game and things were looking good (5 Castles, playing as Green), but had lost a couple of units so was spread thin.

Things went downhill from there, with my territories getting eroded and without sufficient units or any way of getting more units, I don't know what else I could have done beyond praying for a mustering card. 

On the last turn I made a few desperate gambles that had a low chance of success. They failed which should have left me at 2 points, except I made a foolish blunder, leaving me at one point.    

* King-making towards the end. AKA Diplomacy. The player who wins is the one who got picked on the least.
* It's a War-game (I just don't get excited about killing things).
* Players can be out of contention early (though this may be a problem with the players rather than a problem with the game).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Mostly I think this game is pretty amazing. So why a 7? Because Lannister is, IMO, unfun to get stuck with. I get that it's an asymmetrical game. I do! I've played many many asymmetrical games but...damn. Getting stuck with Lannister means you only get to win if someone else's poor play allows it. If nobody's willing to step up and protect you, there is no stopping Greyjoy or Stark from coming through and having your lunch. This leads to the game opening being "solved", at least in our local group, in that Stark must agree to harass Greyjoy (possibly at their own expense) so that Lannister can team up with someone else to beat on Baratheon.

None of this makes any sense if you haven't played it. We play with the expansion and pretty much everything turned on, and if it's a 4p game or a 5p sans Greyjoy, it's all good. It might not be Lannister's problem, truth be told. It might just be that having Greyjoy and Lannister on the same map just sucks. It also feels utterly unlike the books.

Looking forward to the new edition of this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The pace feels about right for what it was. I was thinking all the way through, hmmm:
- movement, units and support rules from Diplomacy
- add resources to supply centres a la Borderlands (& Mare Nostrum & Wallenstein)
- steal the pre-ordering of defined functions from Wallenstein (& crowns=wheat)
- pinch the combat cards from LotR: Confrontation

et voila, some pretty good heritage.

I was expecting more real alliance Diplomacy however, but really it's like any other multi-player wargame where you agree shared boundaries, and pick your time for breaking them to shoot for the win. We don't see real joint alliance play where two players combine to wipe someone out, then move on to the next objective. Maybe that's just our group think though. Too nicey nice.

But if you want to play a bash up the leader multi-player wargame, this played easily and enjoyably. But the experience for me wasn't much different from Mare Nostrum or any of the others.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bad things first:

I'm really disappointed with the quality of the components. Cardboard (all the counters) are of under-average quality, they're not as compressed as the ones made for generally any German publisher, as well as the wood, it's not as good in quality as the German pieces.

Also, the action counters (move, defend, support, etc) share all the same back for each house (the house crest), but they have been printed on one stance on the front side, and some counters from the same house have been printed on the reverse side, this means that if players actually pay attention to detail, they can guess pretty well which actions that particular player is going to use during a turn... most importantly: where!!! That was quite a no no in my book of perfection!

SO FAR I'M NOT CONVINCED ABOUT PRODUCTS MADE IN CHINA. THESE HAVE BEEN OF CLEARLY INFERIOR QUALITY.

As for the game per se, I had a go on a 3-player game and now one more on a 4-player game. You can get a much better idea of what the game is about, but still! This is a 5-player game! The problem with less then 5 players is that there is at least one faction that is going to benefit from the absence of any of the other elements! This gives some advantage.

Still! Loved the tension the game mechanics create. There are very limited resources (meaning especially the planing chips) You look at the board and see dozens of possible options, but know that your action counters are not going to let you do them all, so decision making is very painful. In that regard I loved the game. Nevertheless, I will play it two or three more times (after all the rules are settled) before giving a definite score.

[b]Extra long playing time[/b]
[b]Game for pros![/b]
[b]Back-stab alert![/b]
[b]Strong theme[/b]

RATING: 9.5

Each game is unique and the interaction between players is significant. This is a must try regardless of whether you like the books/show.

RATING: 10
(5+ plays)

Who can play?  Strategy game for 3-6 players.

There is a lot of substance to this game.  Auctions, simultaneous orders, battle cards, and a big beautiful gameboard.  There aren't too many bits to look after, which is admirable for a game of this scope.  Once everyone knows what they are doing, this game can get INTENSE.  This is good, because I can't say that about many games.  And maybe no others that people actually want to play.  There is constant back-stabbing, constantly shifting alliances.  Lot's of thinking about moves, talking players into and out of different moves.  Great strategy options, and yet a simple system with not a single rule question, problem, or argument.

RATING: 8
This used to be one of my all-time favorite games when it first came out.  I still think it's an excellent game and have no intention of upgrading to the second edition.  An elegant marriage of a simple rule set with a surprising depth of strategy.  

That said it's not perfect.  The much-discussed imbalance in starting position (Lannister is all but doomed with five players) and the Westoros decks can be cruel and produce some frustrating game situations if the cards come out in a bad order.

Much to like too, the combat is tense and interesting.  The small number of units you have (combined with the fact you never know how long it will be til more units enter play as well as the Supply track) make this feel very different than most war games.  The hand management aspect lends some interesting situations as well; knowing when and how to lose battles so you can replenish your hand is a difficult tactic to master.

So it has its flaws, but I still love it.

RATING: 6
A new conquest game for me but with familiar elements: card events and programming of your military actions gives an overall Wallenstein feel, with combat resolved a la LotR's Confrontation; as well as conflict from opponents there is a game threat from over the border(similar to Settler's Barbarians/historical scenario) which all players need to contribute power for rewards or suffer consequences for failure. Power is money (which transfers from a reserve to fluid assets like the cabelleros in El Grande) which you use to bid for benefits like going first, plus 1 in a battle, change 1 order. I heard the rumour of diplomacy being a feature so was hesitant in playing the game, but with 3 there was no real sense of looming underhandedness. Maybe interested in playing with more, as there were some interesting movement/avenues of attack, but the diplomacy hook doesn't grabbed me (or the potential elimination).

RATING: 6
A lot of new, more compelling 'dudes on the map' games have come out in the last few years. Although thinking about this game makes me sentimental, ultimately this game always winds up a turtling contest until the last turn when everyone makes their move. Sure, there's usually a little jockeying back and forth for a spot here or there, but the map creates natural defensible borders, and if players start conflicts too early, they always see their neighbor on the other side move in. I suppose that's thematic, but the game is too long an experience for something that really boils down to 2 important turns right at the end.

RATING: 5
This feels like it's so close to being a great game, but it's just missing somethings. The mustering and supply cards are too random, which mean that making plans for future turns isn't that easy. There's no incentive to backstab my "ally" for supply barrels, since the card may never show up again. Further, you have to count on mustering cards showing up at the right time. Ship transport is overpowered. If you lose your access to the seas, you are done in this game. It probably would be a lot better with a Diplomacy-style negotiation phase, but none of the people I've played with want that and barely make any table talk. Since after the 1st turn almost everyone is 2 or 3 cities away from winning, it's hard to identify a leader even though the game turns into a bash-the-leader-fest immediately.

I'd play this again with a group that really got into negotiating and alliances, but then I'd just rather play Diplomacy if it's going to that extent.

RATING: 6.5
An interesting update on the simultaenous action idea of Diplomacy with a spiced up setting that does a lot for A Game of Thrones fans and not much for people who have not read the book (like me), the game mechanisms are easier and more streamlined when compared to Diplomacy but it would be a stretch to call them elegant. The game is balanced not by in-game mechanisms (catch-up) but by the players itself and that is something I don't really like about wargames, and Game of Thrones for that matter. Once a player is behind, he is dependent on the mercy of others if he ever wants to catch up again and even if he does, he will be hard pressed to play a role in the outcome of the game as a whole. Apart from that, an okay game but boiling down a fantasy saga into nothing but a sophisticated risk variant does not really appeal to me. Like all games of this type, I enjoy the company more than the game itself.

RATING: 4
Aprl 2012: Down from 7 to 4. I just don't enjoy playing this. Too long and gamey.

Dec. 2011: Still a 7. It feels a bit gamey (i.e. auctioning for turn order, tie break, and ability to play bonus actions) 

Dec. 2007: Tentative 7 after one play.

Components: Excellent, but the game needs player screens!

Diplomacy-esque game with lots of thematic chrome, which is a good thing.

I admire its design:
* Quick player turns
* Unique economic system: power tokens are used as control markers, to bid for special powers, and one more reason I have forgotten
* Luck free (but not perfect information) combat resolution

Why I probably won't play this game very frequently
* It's a long game
* Game is better with more players, which for me is difficult to find for a game of this length and complexity
* I seem to have a hard time warming up to multi-player war games
* Most importantly, I seem incapable of playing games with lots of special cards and special powers. I have an abstract gamer's mind, and I find it difficult to process the network of relationships between these special powers. Of course, time and experience will remedy this, but there are too many games out there!

RATING: 8

RATING: 9
A very solid conquest game. I'm not into fantasy and don't know the books, but the game feels great anyways. AGOT offers interesting options and diplomacy between players. It also minimizes luck (only in random events and those affect everybody, no dice). I also like the bidding element and the fact that the map doesn't come too crowded because of supply limits. Game plays logically and is quite easy to get hang of, but decisions can be very hard. Sign of a good game =) However, gametime can be quite long if you play with five players and whole 10 turns. The game works best with 5 players though. All game components are top notch. Highly recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I like the combat system in A Game of Thrones.  Basically each player has a hand of special cards that they secretly and simultaneously select when they are in battle with each other which are added to the on board battle strength to determine the winner of a battle.  It's very cool because it's diceless.  There is little randomness or luck, but a player who wins is one who know his/her opponents.  What will the other people do?  There is a lot of economic play in this otherwise strategical war game.  There are auctions for places of power and for advantages.  A Game of Thrones draws on many different game mechanics and it uses them well, which makes it a pretty interesting game to play.  That being sais, I generally prefer to play something with a little less take that!.  As it is a combat and betrayal game, it's not my usual bag.  But it's a very well done game that's not for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I just plain love the tension in this game, the feeling that every choice is difficult because every choice is critical! But after a couple of years of playing it, I've decided that one of the players (House Lannister) is too disadvantaged, and is pretty much guaranteed to have zero chance of winning and a miserable game. (This is true even with the recommended switch of starting positions in the King's Court track.) That makes this game better with four players than with five, because the four-player game improves Lannister's chances considerably.

(Originally rated this a 9; after a few years I reduced it to 8. As much as I admire the design, I've learned that it really is not "my kind" of game. I can rarely get it to the table, and I am no longer very interested in trying. I still enjoy the occasional play when the opportunity arises.)

This game is improved to by the addition of the Ports (which help Lannister some, but not enough) and six-player overlays (optional but wonderful) from the A Clash of Kings expansion.

My full review is here:
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/969567#969567

RATING: 6
Decent entry is the "Risk"  super-family of games.  Good points are the opportunity for cunning with chained moves/attacks, the fixed play time, reasonably good variety in play despite fixed starting positions.  Bad is that the variety introduced through the event deck can result in some dull games with no troops, it is possible that a player will be virtually eliminated from turn 1 or 2.  The game is of lowish complexity and flows smoothly, but depends ultimately (like all these games) on inter-player diplomacy - you'll know whether you like this sort of thing.

RATING: 6
I loved the books and so bought this one.  The very first time we played it ended in under 4 hours, I can see how experienced players can finish in 2 to 3 hours.  I think 3 to 4 hours is ideal for a game so this is perfect for me.  Feels like a much improved diplomacy.  Note in 5 player game Greyjoy can beat Lanister, but the errata fixes it. UPDATE: Lowered my rating to a 7 after a replay.  I now think ships are too powerful.  The map needs more land areas that are not adjacent to the ocean, and River Run should not be adjacent to the western sea (there are cliffs and a small mountain range in the way).  We play that these areas are non-adjacent.
UPDATE: Played again and lowered my rating again, dislike that if you lose your ships on a sea, it is impossible to build up a fleet and come back.  (I'm told that this is fixed in an expansion, but I don't like to have to buy an expansion to fix the basic game.  I'm beginning to think that FFG does not blind test their games.)

Tried writing some house rules to improve this game, gave up and sold it.

RATING: 5
A well designed game that completely fails to capture my interest.  The updated Diplomacy orders system is pretty good and the power tracks are a great idea. However, despite all the cool layers and mechanisms, the game is extremely static.  There are so many limitations to expansion - supply limits, unit limits, combat card limits, mustering limits.  It feels like at the end of the game you've taken maybe three or four neighboring territories over.  There is no feeling of conquest.  The combat system, while I'm sure pleasing to the "no luck" crowd, is simply too dry.  It works better in a short game like LOTR Confrontation.  For a longer game like this, I want some drama in the combat and the game fails to deliver.  The luck is put in the wrong place (removed from combat and put into the Westeros cards), which removes any real excitement from the battles.  I guess overall that would be my complaint with the game - it fails to deliver the level of drama that a game like this should.  If you want a great 3 hour wargame, play Mare Nostrum instead.  If you want a negotiation wargame, this game absolutely pales next to its predecessor Diplomacy.

RATING: 6.5
Take a classic game (pre-1985) and a theme that is popular, paste on top and add a lot of twiddly bits and fancy counters. Knizia has done it with Stratego, and Fury of Dracula riffs on Scotland Yard. This game pastes a popular set of fantasy novels onto Diplomacy. But one of the problems is are you really creating a good new game. This one has a lot of elements from more complex multiplayer strategy/wargames thrown into the mix (mostly it steals from Warrior Knights) but does that really enhance a game like Diplomacy? I'm not entirely convinced, I don't overly like the addition of random elements and military imbalances (supply/attrition/support/CCC) if the games intention is to favour the negotiation element. 
Having said that, its not a bad game, in fact its quite a fun game, but it does feel like you would need to like the theme to say it was a great game.

RATING: 5
Some of the mechanics are neat (simultaneous orders for instance); however, there&#039;s quite a bit I don&#039;t like about the game:

1. decks are too small resulting in too large of a deviation in the draws - especially when it gets shuffled a few times.
2. the way Generals cycle through is a very ugly mechanic - Most of the battles are way too predictable.
3. Once you start losing, you&#039;re on a road of no return and the game is long with 5 players.
4. Not enough player interaction amongst ALL players.

I&#039;ve only played it once but I&#039;d give it one more shot.

I think the game would play better if it were played like Diplomacy with players strategizing together, but there must be a reason I haven&#039;t played Diplomacy in 15 years... PBEM play would improve the game because of the Diplomacy aspect.

RATING: 8
Another crossover wargame.  This one I really enjoy despite knowing next to nothing about the books.  I love the low fantasy feel of it, the hidden orders...just about everything.  I can't wait to play it more to get a better feel for what we should be doing.

UPDATE:  After a six-player game (Martell and the ports only), I'm a little concerned that there isn't much "currency" to use in the negotiations. As usual, Baratheon won after an entire game of nobody doing anything whatsoever to check him.  It's becoming a bit of a tired script.  Still, one of the best Euro/light wargame hybrids out there.

RATING: 8
Good game. Mechanics are pretty tight and elegant. Only a few complaints:
1.) The board design makes it hard to easily see where everyone stands. Territory icons can easily be lost (or hidden) under units. In almost every game I've played the winning player (including myself a couple of times) won because the other players didn't "see" that the player controlled X cities (or that the player had direct access to such territories). Having the territories with strongholds highlighted (entirely or along their borders) would solve this issue.
2.) The map layout and house imbalances make some houses hard to play, especially if you can't negotiate an alliance (I've seen a few games where players will ally and carve up another player's territory - and there isn't much the attacked player can do. Thematically accurate I suppose, but I can't imagine that this is a satisfying gaming experience for the other player).
3.) Army build-up is entirely random (driven by the draw of a card). This creates too many situations where a game can be decided by a fortunate (or unfortunate) "Muster" draw.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
As a dieheart reader of the novel A Song Of Ice And Fire, I have to say I'm a little bit disappointed about AGOT. It copy-paste the mechanic of Diplomacy onto the novel, which only reflects a small part of the essence of GRR Martin's masterpiece. The game manages to stimulate the secret plots and crueless back-stabbing in the novel. However, the game balance, though fixable by diplomacy, is not very good (Greyjoy's fleet over Lannister). And the mechanic of Diplomacy, well, is out of my favour.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A Waro based on the books by George R. R. Martin. Meeple-like troops, streamlined mechanics, limited options, and a bluffing combat card mechanic all mark this as a Euro game with warlike options. Each turn players place order markers, face down, on the map in areas with at least one of your units in it. Orders are things like move, defend, or support and the units in that area can only do what the order says. Spreading out, leaving influence markers behind to retain control of areas you leave, and controlling castles is the main part of the game. The winner is the first player to control 7 castles, and there aren't too many of them on the board, so the game always feels tight, tense, and has your brain constantly working out possibilities. Making temporary alliances, bidding on 3 special tracks, and working together to bid away the wildlings are secondary features of the game. The action cards that are drawn every round are a neat way to spice things up as well. All in all, I'd say this is a solid multiplayer game of conquest.

RATING: 7
A great game with some non-negligible flaws. First and foremost, it's far from balanced with Baratheon winning more than his share of games. This is firstly because of geography of Westeros and initial placement of troops, and secondly due to disproportionately high power of fleets in the game. It can be to some extent remedied by suitable alliances and by actively building ships by oneself, but you still need unbelievably cooperating opponents to win as Stark, and their below average in power house cards don't help here.  The last flaw is possibility (verging on certainity in Stark's case) of getting completely driven out of the sea. This at least was fixed by introduction of ports in the first expansion, which can be easily introduced in the base game - don't play without them!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Multiplayer territorial-conflict game that works beautifully with 5-6 players because of the simultaneous planning (yet it isn't a complete cluster of player-driven chaos like the planning in Shogun). Take regions for power (to bid in auctions for better positioning), for supply (to support larger armies) and so you can place more orders.

A few weaknesses: some houses will just never meet each other on the field (e.g. Martell and Stark in ACoK map); the rich get richer in this game (since they have more orders, supply etc) to the point of player elimination for some; the combat system works elegantly for 2 players like in LotR: The Confrontation, but can reinforce a bash-the-loser mentality in multiplayer; and the events, which give the game replayability, can be a little uneven sometimes by controlling the timing of such crucial actions as resetting supply and mustering units. Runewars, a game obviously inspired by this game, deals with the last point by making harvesting and recruiting player-driven, and it's a lot more satisfying that way (ACoK also helps with the muster special order).

More on the similarities with Runewars:
 - Power = Influence
 - Routing Units
 - Iron Throne = Primarch of the Wizards' Council
 - Fiefdoms = Commander of the Warriors' Guild
 - Same victory conditions
 - Westeros deck = Season Events
 - Supply = Winter starvation

Also, I feel really sad about the wooden bits. They're functional, but a game this richly infused with a lurid theme needs to look a lot sexier.

One more: the theme is not strongly integrated. The artwork helps convey the setting, but the abstraction in the game does not glue it to the mechanisms. The only attempt is putting character names in the House Cards, but they are forgettably similar, especially the ones that just have sword and fortification icons. Also, one cannot anoint this as a 'negotiation game' or emblematic of the intrigue in the source material when [i]you can't even trade your power tokens[/i]! Seriously considering that as a house rule.

And another: the rich get richer in this game. You can be stuck without a way to win for a loooong time.

Design is a little lower, on the 3 side, due to balance issues. Subsequent expansions and re-issues (Ports, Greyjoy tweak) help to address this. It is still rich-get-richer, however. Very fun when you're contending for the win, and total lameness when you're stuck in no-man's land.

3+3

RATING: 9
I thought this was an intriguing game, but my chums didn't care for it a whole lot and felt it was too long, but I thought it was pretty cool. A wargame without dice is a fascinating concept. Its a wargame where the focus isn't on numbers, its on the interaction between players (backstabbing and the like). Fascinating.

Since writing that blurb years ago, my appreciation for this game has only grown. The capabilty for player interaction is great. Note, people who are good at manipulating people and/or bluffing will have a large advantage in this game.

The cards cause this game to play differently every time; usually this results in not having enough musters, supply calcuations, or bidding wars come up; I had one game where I passed on bidding the first turn and it literally *never* came up again - I was stuck in last place the whole game. This mechanic is at the same time a strength (different gameplay each play) and a weakness (its diffcult to plan strategy when you have no idea what will come up in the future).

RATING: 8.7
I think this game would become dramatically better with a steady play group.  Players new to AGoT will possibly lead to a boring game.  I like the way the combat system is set up so that casualties aren't too heavy.  There are some notable balance issues amongst the various factions.  With some tweaking I think the balance problems could be resolved.  The majority of the problem lies with House Greyjoy in the 5 player game as it essentially puts House Lannister in a vice from the start.  

A better option might be to make Greyjoy a neutral 6 and to make the Eyrie a playable faction instead.  This would take the heavy pressure off of Lannister and add pressure to House Baratheon (which is probably the strongest faction in a 5 player game.)  The board would have to undergo some modifications as well (in regards to city and stronghold positioning).  Of course, this doesn't follow the story in the books, but I think it would make for a better game.  If I remember correctly House Greyjoy didn't jump into the frey until quite later anyway.

RATING: 6
[2011-08-06]
Szép kivitelű, magyarul is megjelent játék. Régóta szerettem volna kipróbálni, mert már sok jót hallottam róla. A játék alapötletéül szolgáló regényről semmit nem hallottam így a háttértörténetről nem írnék. A lényeg, hogy különböző családok harcolnak a trónért Westeros területén. A játékosok területeket hódítanak meg, minél többet a várral rendelkező tartományok közül, hiszen a cél 7 ilyen tartomány megszerezése, ha ez senkinek nem sikerül a 10. kör után a legtöbbel rendelkező család nyer. A harc simán többségi, illetve mindkét fél pontosan ugyanolyan kártyapaklijából egy pluszt kijátszva növelheti az erejét. A kártyák közül mindig egyet lehet használni és mindet el kell használni, hogy újra az összeset a kézbe lehessen venni. Igazából semmi gond nincs ezzel a harcrendszerrel, mivel a kártyák maximum hármas erejűek lehetnek, így az erősebb fél, ha ennél nagyobb erővel támad biztosra mehet. A legyőzött egységek visszavonulnak, megsemmisíteni őket nehezen lehet és 1-2 maximum. Így nagyon nehéz valakit ellehetetleníteni a játékban teljesen, egyedül talán a tengeren lehet, mert támogatás nem jöhet csak üres tengerrészre, ha pedig ilyen nincs akkor nem tud újra hajót szerezni az adott ház. Minden területen, ha egység áll a játékosok lefordított parancsjelzőket helyeznek el, amivel mozogni/támadni, védekezni, támogatni, más parancsjelzőt megsemmisíteni, illetve befolyásjelzőket szerezni tudnak. A játékban történnek különböző események és csatán túl befolyásjelzők segítségével három területen tudunk minél előrébb állni. A három terület a parancsjelzők végrehajtási sorrendjét, a csatában a döntetlen eldöntését és különleges parancsjelzők mennyiségének a számát határozzák meg. A különböző családok harci kártyáikban és kezdőfelállásukban térnek el egymástól, de tulajdonképpen ez utóbbi fontosabb. Van kedvezőbb helyzetben levő család, de ha tapasztalt játékosok ülnek le a játékhoz úgy gondolom jól kompenzálható ez. Tulajdonképpen semmibe nem tudok belekötni a játékkal kapcsolatban. Még a játékidőbe sem, amit sokan negatívumként írnak, mert az első játék nekünk nem tartott sokáig, igaz ez főleg a tapasztalatlanságunknak köszönhető. Amúgy pedig akár kevesebb köröst is lehet játszani, ha valakinek hosszú lenne. A Trónok harca egy szép kivitelű, jó játék, de valahogy nem fogott meg. Mondjuk nem is igazán szeretem a kimondott harcközpontú játékokat, a TH pedig ilyen, máshogy nem is lehet nyerni benne, de aki kedveli az ilyeneket szerintem nem fog csalódni benne.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
When it works the system is intriguing, when it doesn't the game is dull. In the golden hour, everyone is in contention and it is difficult to get anything accomplished unless you have someone backing you up, ala Diplomacy.  I enjoy this much more than Dip though in that things actually have to happen rather than just lying to each other and just bouncing over and over again.  But when things lag, it is often best just to turtle and collect chits.  

This game does suffer from what I feel is worse than elimination, being beaten into submission, with no way out, and pretending to play the game until someone mercifully eliminates you.  Though at that point you aren't worth the effort, ugh.  

I've got to say that the red faction sucks.  It is just way too easy to get the Malachi Crunch.  The revamped suggestions help a little, but not much.  As usual Fantasy Flight fixes the most problematic part of the game in the expansion--The Port.  But, I feel that the expansion ruins the balance with 8 cards. My suggestion, get a couple round pieces of cardboard and use the port rules online.

The game itself is a bit dry and mathy, and you can see yourself getting crushed a mile away without being able to do anything worthwhile.  It could use a little luck in the battles for my taste.  Contrary to that there is a ton of luck in the mustering/supply phase.  I played one game where only one muster came up (in the 6th round) for the whole game.  Dullsville.  Flukey games are not that outlandish.

It is just too static for my tastes, despite having neat ideas.

RATING: 5
I was really looking forward to this game, strategic play with a theme I love (read the books). Although I had some doubts, because the books are pretty dark fantasy. There is a lot of cutthroat stuff, betrayal and the more you get into it, the less "good characters" you find. It's a political story. So is the game. I think what bothers me most about the game is the political element, you have to make partnerships, offering things and hoping the other person won't stab you in your back when you need him. I'm just not into such a thing. Which would be fine if you could do without it. You can't. If you try to play on your turf, not messing with anyone and just try to make allies in key situations, you're going to lose probably. It's not a bad game and I figure someone who likes negotiations will have a blast with this game. If you don't like tight negotiations that can backfire, you probably won't like it.

RATING: 9.5
About as perfectly executed military / pseudo-euro strategy game I've ever played.  Awesome stuff.

Have yet to grow tired of it after many plays, and still ache to get it on the table more often.

Edit 12/09/09:  Too many people THINK they like GoT, or WANT to like GoT, and just can't admit that it's not a game for them.  If you're prone to AP, this is probably NOT the game for you.  I've played 9 games thus far this year (year almost over) and only 4 have been played to their conclusion though most all have lasted at least 4 hours.  Not a good track record.  Many players either a) aren't decisive and scared to make an error, or b) can't focus when it isn't their turn, and have to be reminded constantly what's going on.  For me, it's still an awesome game... just exercise caution with whom you're sitting down to play.

RATING: 7
Loving the theme helps a lot.

This suffers if the players are at different levels, as it punishes bad moves harshly.

House rule: you have a diplomatic phase before putting down your orders. 5 min to discuss. After this you are -not- allowed to discuss anymore, even after revealing the orders. Speeds up the game, more focus on diplomacy. This made the game more enjoyable for me, it really worked.

We still talk about sessions of this, months later. It has to be at least a 7, just for that.

RATING: 6
I've played one game and I liked it. However, it comes with some snafus:

1. It's a bit long and slow-moving. I have pretty decent patience with games and I can handle long games. So I didn't mind this much, I was so engaged the whole time that the time slipped by. The problem for me with this, however, is that most my gaming friends and family, don't have the same patience I do.

2. In the game I played, Lannister was pounced by GreyJoy in the first turn and never recovered. Lannister had no chance. I have read a bit that there are fixes to help Lannister's starting position in order to balance this out. But this was a really bad thing for the Lannister player in my first game.

3. No ports in the base game. I'm glad FFG is coming out with a new edition that addresses this issue, because I can see how it would have been a huge problem--we played with the expansion that adds ports.

4. Westeros cards can be too random. I realize this is what makes every game different and unpredictable, and I think that's a good thing. However, the Westeros cards were very cruel in our first game. We mustered twice and bid for power positioning twice in the first two turns. After that, never again for the rest of the game. GreyJoy was able to pull off a 7-castle victory in turn 6 because its position mustering power and supply in turn two was double what most of us had, again, only because it was so easy for her to take over Lannister's home land. If a single mustering had occured in rounds 3-6, I'm certain the rest of us could have stopped GreyJoy from the sweeping win. But the way the cards played, we never had a chance.

All in all, a good game. And I think it accomplishes that for which it was created: a war game with much intrigue and diplomacy. But because of these few flaws I see, I think I'll hold off on purchasing it. At least until I have a group of players who I'm sure would enjoy it.

RATING: N/A
Excellent diplomacy game. It has terrific concepts, plays differently every time, it has varying factions and no luck. You need to outplay, outsmart and deceive your opponents while at the same time negotiating so as to do it first before them.

The only drawback I can think of is that the factions have standard starting positions, and therefore the first turns can perhaps be easily scripted and some alliances appear more often than not. But it really is a minor issue.

I expect that my rating will rise the more I play.


Update: The second edition of this game is superior in every regard, and has made the first edition obsolete for me. This is still a great game, but since I'll never choose to play it again I have removed my rating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is a perfect example of a light tactical/strategic wargame.  It's mechanics create a system of combat that involves no luck where the victor is the one who managed his forces and alliances most effectively.  A cunning leader can easily determine whether a battle is impossible to win in any given scenario.  This separates it from the vast majority of light wargames out there - ex: A&A - which rely mainly on dice for their combat mechanics.  I think its a very clever game that deserves more attention than it's gotten.  The expansions that FF is putting out look like they are very high quality and I'm very glad that they're supporting the game further into it's lifespan.

RATING: 9.5
I like this game. I love the theme and the source material. I extremely enjoy the non-dice approach to battle resolution. It just makes sense. There is a lot going on in this game (bidding, resource allocation, diplomacy, outside threats from the wildlings, and so on) that might make it confusing when you read through the rules. However, if you play it once, you will see that the game flows very orderly and allows an optimal use of all these mechanics to be used. 

I'd rate this game a 10 except for the play time. I usually avoid games that take over two hours. This one can take up to four or five hours if playing with a competitive group. Quality over quantity I know but that type of time frame for playing really impacts how often the game would hit the table with my group.

EDITED: Bumped this up to 9.5. If you get a group of people who are familiar with the game, the play time is reduced substantially. Also, that same familiarity in a group adds to interesting alliances that build upon the theme. Its a great game that keeps getting better in my mind.

RATING: 4
Potentially great game, but the order in which events take place has decided my fate twice. Once no reinforcements showed up three rounds in a row, the second play I kept ridiculous stacking limits. Spoils the game somewhat.
Another annoying mechanism is that of the house-cards: a limited deck which has to be used completely before cards come into play again. Because it takes a couple of turns before you get your cards back, you'll be left with probably some lame cards.
Conclusion: if my gaming group wants to bring this to the table again, I'll probably join but I'll never suggest it. Too many games I like better, too little time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Five player wargame based on the famous series of fantasy novels. Interesting mechanics that effectively reduce the randomness usually present in such offerings. No dice! The  meat of the game revolves around a cleaver planning phase where players simultaneously place hidden orders on the board (revealed later in the turn). Factions have some uniqueness provided by character cards and starting positions. An overall very well designed game that unfortunately seems prone to analysis paralysis - especially in the planning phase. An extensive amount of diplomacy can also lengthen the game. The length is further problematic considering that one or two of the houses will invariably be repressed to very weak positions early on and be non-factors in the course of the game unless their fortunes turn (but whether it does is likely to be out of their hands). Wouldn't mind playing again but would appreciate some mechanism that speeds up game decisions to improve the flow and reduce overall length.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is the game that got me into the board gaming hobby.  It was "Risk without dice" which was the perfect concept for me. In addition, it has a good theme, great tactical elements, mind-gaming non-dice combat resolutions and an excellent bidding mechanism for some key gameplay advantages.  

In short, AGOT has everything I look for in a game - little to no luck, conflict, diplomacy, grand strategy, tactics and bluffing. Only drawback is the time commitment - this game often runs at 3+ hours due to the huge amounts of AP during the order phase. We've managed to bring that down a bit by putting in a time limit of 7 minutes for orders and 2 orders for all other actions (marching and card selecting).

One drawback - this game is lacking without the Clash of Kings expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Sweet baby Jeebus, but this is a game with a lot going on.  It's not hard, and the turns are pretty straightforward, but everything to consider, all the choices to make, and how things will change from one seemingly small little move add up to a big game with lots of confrontation and shifting alliances.  And I really respect a game with that kind of a design, with the agonizing choices and having to live with the results of a miscalculated move.  The problem is that these aren't the types of games I like or want to play.

For one, I tend to do very poorly at them.  I don't know if it's my misunderstanding of sacrificing units or just a lack of strategic thinking when it comes to these sorts of invasion games, but I can't seem to get my head around how to maintain a wide enough, strong enough force to make a difference in these games.  I tend to feel more frustrated than anything else, and that's not a feeling I want to engender too much when it comes to games.

2 plays: 5 players

RATING: 10
OK. So I played Diplomacy ONCE in my lifetime, and I absolutely loved it. And here I wax hypocritical by saying that the only thing that has prohibited me from playing more of that perennial classic is its length - everyone who knows me knows that TIME is just about the last consideration I give when deciding to play a game. Still, I don't play as much Diplomacy as I'd like. And although AGOT doesn't approach the complexity and richness of the classic to which it gets compared, I don't care. It's my Diplomacy quick fix. I like AGOT because it reminds me of Diplomacy. I like AGOT because it practically eliminates the random factor that can get annoying in Euros or dice-based games. I like AGOT because I like George R.R. Martin's fantasy setting. I like AGOT because stoned b****es can't gang up on you for no apparent reason whatsoever. And I like AGOT because Robert Martin doesn't.

Toch, als dit spel voorgesteld word zal ik het met plezier spelen, vooral omdat de boeken ook zo leuk zijn.

I still love the game after all this years but finally I realized that it is not a 10 anymore. It is a bit too long and too intense to be played regularly. I tend to think of it as an event game (although it is not THAT long)

RATING: 8
A classic multiplayer attack  (and effect) the other players "80's style" game with a touch of elements of today's games.  Gamers new to gaming could learn alot about how to play games with alliances if they can play this enough to develop a feel for reading how close each player is to victory. (Note that as a "multiplayer combat game" initial forces don't have to be balanced since combat will shift the balance anyway.  Learn to negotiate to win. Create alliances. Most importantly: unlike most Eurogames, don't think you can win by playing the game mechanics alone.)

RATING: 5.5
Yet to play, but according to a friend of mine really, really fun, though really, really long.

[UPDATE]: Played from 04/03/13 20:30 to 05/03/13 02:00. It was pretty fun, although no one really had a clue about what was the best thing to do. Biggest mistake: ignoring Lannister (perhaps because she was the only girl around the table...), which meant that on turns 8 & 9, she had nearly won by capturing 7 strongholds. Lucky us, she didn't have any Power Tokens to keep areas with strongholds under her control as she moved out. Not that it mattered anyway, as at the end of turn 10 she had the most strongholds... 

[UPDATE 2]: Played twice July 6th. The first game ended after two turns, the second one after three. It really showed me and the people I played with how punishing this game is: if one player makes just one sub-optimal play, someone else can instantly take advantage of that and take the win while none of the other three players can do anything about it. Especially Stark (and Tyrell to a lesser extent), who is less involved with the game since they can't get straight into the action. This makes it difficult for Stark and Tyrell to grab cities and strongholds, since the other three players will already have taken those by the time they actually get to do anything. The fact that there are precise starting positions for each of the houses and that they always have the same house cards doesn't help things along when it comes to replayability. In both games we played this day, there were about 2/3 battles in both games. That's not very exciting. I don't know to what extent the 2nd edition is better, but in the current state, I wouldn't suggest to play this 1st edition myself, dropping my 6,5 rating to a 5,5.

RATING: 8

If taken too seriously one could be frustrated, but a eurogamer mentality that can handle a bit of randomness with likely enjoy this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
+: epic strategy game
+: tense & based on a good book series with much background info
+: unique (although it might have stolen the best parts of several games)
+: interactive/conflict-rich: cut-throat area movement, verbal and sneaky diplomacy (backstabbing allowed), leader-bashing, player chaos
+: little randomness (except for player order (bid?) and event cards (variant))
+: predict the choices of other players
+: several officially promoted variants (some are free)
+: others having AP affects game length in a small way
-: can pose a steep learning curve to non-gamers
-: you can be "out of the game" if you played poorly (or got unlucky) and may need to sit it out (though I have also seen come-backs)
-: kingmaking possible (overcome this by enforcing that a non-winning possible kingmaker must make the move that is best for him))
-: unbalanced: black greyjoy is too strong (needs docks (for all players) and downgrades to raven's court and knight to be playable) and green Tyrell is often too weak
-: needs expansion (clash of kings, $) for balance: docks are vital, other variants are nice for variation
-: needs expansion (clash of kings, $) for 6
-: not playable with 4 or less (unless with a variant I don't know? (I don't like Storm of Swords))

RATING: 6
This game LOOKS like it should be much more fun than it actually is. The components are pretty and the rules are intriguing, but in the end it just comes off as a bit disappointing. Combat is not particularly interesting, and my biggest pet peeve is that units DO NOT die. It is nearly impossible to kill a large number of enemy units. Also, when the game ends it is sudden and I remain unsatisfied with the result.

EDIT: Played again with four people. A lot more dialogue keeps people honest, and as soon as someone hits 5 cities it becomes a "beat the leader" mentality. Still some issues with the game, but much more fun than I originally felt it was.

RATING: 7
Стратегия как её себе представляет каждый игрок. Есть все уровни - снабжение, подкрепления, поддержка, морские перевозки, удержание территорий. И всё это без кубиков, на основе европейских механик (ареа контрол, закрытый аукцион и т.п.). Единственный минус, почему не 9 или 10 - долгая, с новчиками часа 4-5 из-за даунтайма, с профессионалами столько же из-за возросшего умения.

RATING: 6
I like games that try to pick up where Diplomacy left off, but seems to have many balance issues and AP problems. At least it's shorter than Diplomacy. But it seems like Diplomacy plus a lot of work, which still ultimately comes down the diplomacy part.

Having the Supply event not happen often, and especially if it happens late the first time, can seriously affect the game.

One of the gamers who we play with has read the books the game is based on and says that the game is good at recreating the stetting of the books, who has power, who allies with who etc.

RATING: 7
Best mix of different but excellent mechanisms. Some balancing problem and in particular too much dependent on the alliances; players are always in a vulnerable position, making it easy to gang up on the leader, and ultimately hard to come up with an effective strategy.
